In the simplest case,\u00a0__init__.py<\/span><\/code>\u00a0can just be an empty file, but it can also execute initialization code for the package or set the\u00a0__all__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0variable, described later.<\/p>
                  Users of the package can import individual modules from the package, for example:<\/p>
                  import<\/span> sound.effects.echo<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  This loads the submodule\u00a0sound.effects.echo<\/span><\/code>. It must be referenced with its full name.<\/p>
                  sound<\/span>.<\/span>effects<\/span>.<\/span>echo<\/span>.<\/span>echofilter<\/span>(<\/span>input<\/span>,<\/span> output<\/span>,<\/span> delay<\/span>=<\/span>0.7<\/span>,<\/span> atten<\/span>=<\/span>4<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  An alternative way of importing the submodule is:<\/p>
                  from<\/span> sound.effects<\/span> import<\/span> echo<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  This also loads the submodule\u00a0echo<\/span><\/code>, and makes it available without its package prefix, so it can be used as follows:<\/p>
                  echo<\/span>.<\/span>echofilter<\/span>(<\/span>input<\/span>,<\/span> output<\/span>,<\/span> delay<\/span>=<\/span>0.7<\/span>,<\/span> atten<\/span>=<\/span>4<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  Yet another variation is to import the desired function or variable directly:<\/p>
                  from<\/span> sound.effects.echo<\/span> import<\/span> echofilter<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  Again, this loads the submodule\u00a0echo<\/span><\/code>, but this makes its function\u00a0echofilter()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0directly available:<\/p>
                  echofilter<\/span>(<\/span>input<\/span>,<\/span> output<\/span>,<\/span> delay<\/span>=<\/span>0.7<\/span>,<\/span> atten<\/span>=<\/span>4<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  Note that when using\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0package<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0item<\/span><\/code>, the item can be either a submodule (or subpackage) of the package, or some other name defined in the package, like a function, class or variable. The\u00a0import<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement first tests whether the item is defined in the package; if not, it assumes it is a module and attempts to load it. If it fails to find it, an\u00a0ImportError<\/span><\/code>\u00a0exception is raised.<\/p>
                  Contrarily, when using syntax like\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0item.subitem.subsubitem<\/span><\/code>, each item except for the last must be a package; the last item can be a module or a package but can\u2019t be a class or function or variable defined in the previous item.<\/p>
                  \u00a0<\/p>
                  6.4.1.\u00a0<\/span>Importing * From a Package<\/h3>
                  Now what happens when the user writes\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0sound.effects<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0*<\/span><\/code>? Ideally, one would hope that this somehow goes out to the filesystem, finds which submodules are present in the package, and imports them all. This could take a long time and importing sub-modules might have unwanted side-effects that should only happen when the sub-module is explicitly imported.<\/p>
                  The only solution is for the package author to provide an explicit index of the package. The\u00a0import<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement uses the following convention: if a package\u2019s\u00a0__init__.py<\/span><\/code>\u00a0code defines a list named\u00a0__all__<\/span><\/code>, it is taken to be the list of module names that should be imported when\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0package<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0*<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is encountered. It is up to the package author to keep this list up-to-date when a new version of the package is released. Package authors may also decide not to support it, if they don\u2019t see a use for importing * from their package. For example, the file\u00a0sound\/effects\/__init__.py<\/span><\/code>\u00a0could contain the following code:<\/p>
                  __all__<\/span> =<\/span> [<\/span>\"echo\"<\/span>,<\/span> \"surround\"<\/span>,<\/span> \"reverse\"<\/span>]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  This would mean that\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0sound.effects<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0*<\/span><\/code>\u00a0would import the three named submodules of the\u00a0sound<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package.<\/p>
                  If\u00a0__all__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is not defined, the statement\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0sound.effects<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0*<\/span><\/code>\u00a0does\u00a0not<\/em>\u00a0import all submodules from the package\u00a0sound.effects<\/span><\/code>\u00a0into the current namespace; it only ensures that the package\u00a0sound.effects<\/span><\/code>\u00a0has been imported (possibly running any initialization code in\u00a0__init__.py<\/span><\/code>) and then imports whatever names are defined in the package. This includes any names defined (and submodules explicitly loaded) by\u00a0__init__.py<\/span><\/code>. It also includes any submodules of the package that were explicitly loaded by previous\u00a0import<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statements. Consider this code:<\/p>
                  import<\/span> sound.effects.echo<\/span>\nimport<\/span> sound.effects.surround<\/span>\nfrom<\/span> sound.effects<\/span> import<\/span> *<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  In this example, the\u00a0echo<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0surround<\/span><\/code>\u00a0modules are imported in the current namespace because they are defined in the\u00a0sound.effects<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package when the\u00a0from...import<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement is executed. (This also works when\u00a0__all__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is defined.)<\/p>
                  Although certain modules are designed to export only names that follow certain patterns when you use\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0*<\/span><\/code>, it is still considered bad practice in production code.<\/p>
                  Remember, there is nothing wrong with using\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0package<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0specific_submodule<\/span><\/code>! In fact, this is the recommended notation unless the importing module needs to use submodules with the same name from different packages.<\/p><\/div>
                  6.4.2.\u00a0<\/span>Intra-package References<\/h3>
                  When packages are structured into subpackages (as with the\u00a0sound<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package in the example), you can use absolute imports to refer to submodules of siblings packages. For example, if the module\u00a0sound.filters.vocoder<\/span><\/code>\u00a0needs to use the\u00a0echo<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module in the\u00a0sound.effects<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package, it can use\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0sound.effects<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0echo<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                  You can also write relative imports, with the\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0module<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0name<\/span><\/code>\u00a0form of import statement. These imports use leading dots to indicate the current and parent packages involved in the relative import. From the\u00a0surround<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module for example, you might use:<\/p>
                  from<\/span> .<\/span> import<\/span> echo<\/span>\nfrom<\/span> ..<\/span> import<\/span> formats<\/span>\nfrom<\/span> ..filters<\/span> import<\/span> equalizer<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                  Note that relative imports are based on the name of the current module. Since the name of the main module is always\u00a0\"__main__\"<\/span><\/code>, modules intended for use as the main module of a Python application must always use absolute imports.<\/p><\/div>
                  6.4.3.\u00a0<\/span>Packages in Multiple Directories<\/h3>
                  Packages support one more special attribute,\u00a0__path__<\/span><\/code>. This is initialized to be a list containing the name of the directory holding the package\u2019s\u00a0__init__.py<\/span><\/code>\u00a0before the code in that file is executed. This variable can be modified; doing so affects future searches for modules and subpackages contained in the package.<\/p>
                  While this feature is not often needed, it can be used to extend the set of modules found in a package.<\/p><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                  \n\t\t\t\t\t
                  7.\u00a0<\/span>Input and Output<\/h2>
                  There are several ways to present the output of a program; data can be printed in a human-readable form, or written to a file for future use. This chapter will discuss some of the possibilities.<\/p>
                  \u00a0<\/p>
                  7.1.\u00a0<\/span>Fancier Output Formatting<\/h2>
                  So far we\u2019ve encountered two ways of writing values:\u00a0expression statements<\/em>\u00a0and the\u00a0print()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function. (A third way is using the\u00a0write()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method of file objects; the standard output file can be referenced as\u00a0sys.stdout<\/span><\/code>. See the Library Reference for more information on this.)<\/p>
                  Often you\u2019ll want more control over the formatting of your output than simply printing space-separated values. There are several ways to format output.<\/p>
                  • To use\u00a0formatted string literals<\/span>, begin a string with\u00a0f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0F<\/span><\/code>\u00a0before the opening quotation mark or triple quotation mark. Inside this string, you can write a Python expression between\u00a0{<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0}<\/span><\/code>\u00a0characters that can refer to variables or literal values.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>year<\/span> =<\/span> 2016<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>event<\/span> =<\/span> 'Referendum'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>'Results of the <\/span>{<\/span>year<\/span>}<\/span> {<\/span>event<\/span>}<\/span>'<\/span>\n'Results of the 2016 Referendum'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/li>
                  • The\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method of strings requires more manual effort. You\u2019ll still use\u00a0{<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0}<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to mark where a variable will be substituted and can provide detailed formatting directives, but you\u2019ll also need to provide the information to be formatted.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>yes_votes<\/span> =<\/span> 42_572_654<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>no_votes<\/span> =<\/span> 43_132_495<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>percentage<\/span> =<\/span> yes_votes<\/span> \/<\/span> (<\/span>yes_votes<\/span> +<\/span> no_votes<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>'<\/span>{:-9}<\/span> YES votes  <\/span>{:2.2%}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>yes_votes<\/span>,<\/span> percentage<\/span>)<\/span>\n' 42572654 YES votes  49.67%'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/li>
                  • Finally, you can do all the string handling yourself by using string slicing and concatenation operations to create any layout you can imagine. The string type has some methods that perform useful operations for padding strings to a given column width.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                    When you don\u2019t need fancy output but just want a quick display of some variables for debugging purposes, you can convert any value to a string with the\u00a0repr()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0str()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0functions.<\/p>
                    The\u00a0str()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function is meant to return representations of values which are fairly human-readable, while\u00a0repr()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is meant to generate representations which can be read by the interpreter (or will force a\u00a0SyntaxError<\/span><\/code>\u00a0if there is no equivalent syntax). For objects which don\u2019t have a particular representation for human consumption,\u00a0str()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will return the same value as\u00a0repr()<\/span><\/code>. Many values, such as numbers or structures like lists and dictionaries, have the same representation using either function. Strings, in particular, have two distinct representations.<\/p>
                    Some examples:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>s<\/span> =<\/span> 'Hello, world.'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>str<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n'Hello, world.'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>repr<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n\"'Hello, world.'\"<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>str<\/span>(<\/span>1<\/span>\/<\/span>7<\/span>)<\/span>\n'0.14285714285714285'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>x<\/span> =<\/span> 10<\/span> *<\/span> 3.25<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>y<\/span> =<\/span> 200<\/span> *<\/span> 200<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>s<\/span> =<\/span> 'The value of x is '<\/span> +<\/span> repr<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span> +<\/span> ', and y is '<\/span> +<\/span> repr<\/span>(<\/span>y<\/span>)<\/span> +<\/span> '...'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\nThe value of x is 32.5, and y is 40000...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span># The repr() of a string adds string quotes and backslashes:<\/span>\n... <\/span>hello<\/span> =<\/span> 'hello, world<\/span>\\n<\/span>'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>hellos<\/span> =<\/span> repr<\/span>(<\/span>hello<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>hellos<\/span>)<\/span>\n'hello, world\\n'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span># The argument to repr() may be any Python object:<\/span>\n... <\/span>repr<\/span>((<\/span>x<\/span>,<\/span> y<\/span>,<\/span> (<\/span>'spam'<\/span>,<\/span> 'eggs'<\/span>)))<\/span>\n\"(32.5, 40000, ('spam', 'eggs'))\"<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    The\u00a0string<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module contains a\u00a0Template<\/span><\/code>\u00a0class that offers yet another way to substitute values into strings, using placeholders like\u00a0$x<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and replacing them with values from a dictionary, but offers much less control of the formatting.<\/p>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    7.1.1.\u00a0<\/span>Formatted String Literals<\/h3>
                    Formatted string literals<\/span>\u00a0(also called f-strings for short) let you include the value of Python expressions inside a string by prefixing the string with\u00a0f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0F<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and writing expressions as\u00a0{expression}<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                    An optional format specifier can follow the expression. This allows greater control over how the value is formatted. The following example rounds pi to three places after the decimal:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>import<\/span> math<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>f<\/span>'The value of pi is approximately <\/span>{<\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>pi<\/span>:<\/span>.3f<\/span>}<\/span>.'<\/span>)<\/span>\nThe value of pi is approximately 3.142.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    Passing an integer after the\u00a0':'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will cause that field to be a minimum number of characters wide. This is useful for making columns line up.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>table<\/span> =<\/span> {<\/span>'Sjoerd'<\/span>:<\/span> 4127<\/span>,<\/span> 'Jack'<\/span>:<\/span> 4098<\/span>,<\/span> 'Dcab'<\/span>:<\/span> 7678<\/span>}<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>for<\/span> name<\/span>,<\/span> phone<\/span> in<\/span> table<\/span>.<\/span>items<\/span>():<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>f<\/span>'<\/span>{<\/span>name<\/span>:<\/span>10<\/span>}<\/span> ==> <\/span>{<\/span>phone<\/span>:<\/span>10d<\/span>}<\/span>'<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nSjoerd     ==>       4127<\/span>\nJack       ==>       4098<\/span>\nDcab       ==>       7678<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    Other modifiers can be used to convert the value before it is formatted.\u00a0'!a'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0applies\u00a0ascii()<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0'!s'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0applies\u00a0str()<\/span><\/code>, and\u00a0'!r'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0applies\u00a0repr()<\/span><\/code>:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>animals<\/span> =<\/span> 'eels'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>f<\/span>'My hovercraft is full of <\/span>{<\/span>animals<\/span>}<\/span>.'<\/span>)<\/span>\nMy hovercraft is full of eels.<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>f<\/span>'My hovercraft is full of <\/span>{<\/span>animals<\/span>!r}<\/span>.'<\/span>)<\/span>\nMy hovercraft is full of 'eels'.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    For a reference on these format specifications, see the reference guide for the\u00a0Format Specification Mini-Language<\/span>.<\/p><\/div>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    7.1.2.\u00a0<\/span>The String format() Method<\/h3>
                    Basic usage of the\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method looks like this:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'We are the <\/span>{}<\/span> who say \"<\/span>{}<\/span>!\"'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>'knights'<\/span>,<\/span> 'Ni'<\/span>))<\/span>\nWe are the knights who say \"Ni!\"<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    The brackets and characters within them (called format fields) are replaced with the objects passed into the\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method. A number in the brackets can be used to refer to the position of the object passed into the\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'<\/span>{0}<\/span> and <\/span>{1}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>'spam'<\/span>,<\/span> 'eggs'<\/span>))<\/span>\nspam and eggs<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'<\/span>{1}<\/span> and <\/span>{0}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>'spam'<\/span>,<\/span> 'eggs'<\/span>))<\/span>\neggs and spam<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    If keyword arguments are used in the\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method, their values are referred to by using the name of the argument.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'This <\/span>{food}<\/span> is <\/span>{adjective}<\/span>.'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>\n... <\/span>      food<\/span>=<\/span>'spam'<\/span>,<\/span> adjective<\/span>=<\/span>'absolutely horrible'<\/span>))<\/span>\nThis spam is absolutely horrible.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    Positional and keyword arguments can be arbitrarily combined:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'The story of <\/span>{0}<\/span>, <\/span>{1}<\/span>, and <\/span>{other}<\/span>.'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>'Bill'<\/span>,<\/span> 'Manfred'<\/span>,<\/span>\n                                                       other='Georg'))<\/span>\nThe story of Bill, Manfred, and Georg.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    If you have a really long format string that you don\u2019t want to split up, it would be nice if you could reference the variables to be formatted by name instead of by position. This can be done by simply passing the dict and using square brackets\u00a0'[]'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to access the keys.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>table<\/span> =<\/span> {<\/span>'Sjoerd'<\/span>:<\/span> 4127<\/span>,<\/span> 'Jack'<\/span>:<\/span> 4098<\/span>,<\/span> 'Dcab'<\/span>:<\/span> 8637678<\/span>}<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'Jack: <\/span>{0[Jack]:d}<\/span>; Sjoerd: <\/span>{0[Sjoerd]:d}<\/span>; '<\/span>\n... <\/span>      'Dcab: <\/span>{0[Dcab]:d}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>table<\/span>))<\/span>\nJack: 4098; Sjoerd: 4127; Dcab: 8637678<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    This could also be done by passing the table as keyword arguments with the \u2018**\u2019 notation.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>table<\/span> =<\/span> {<\/span>'Sjoerd'<\/span>:<\/span> 4127<\/span>,<\/span> 'Jack'<\/span>:<\/span> 4098<\/span>,<\/span> 'Dcab'<\/span>:<\/span> 8637678<\/span>}<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'Jack: <\/span>{Jack:d}<\/span>; Sjoerd: <\/span>{Sjoerd:d}<\/span>; Dcab: <\/span>{Dcab:d}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>**<\/span>table<\/span>))<\/span>\nJack: 4098; Sjoerd: 4127; Dcab: 8637678<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    This is particularly useful in combination with the built-in function\u00a0vars()<\/span><\/code>, which returns a dictionary containing all local variables.<\/p>
                    As an example, the following lines produce a tidily-aligned set of columns giving integers and their squares and cubes:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>for<\/span> x<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 11<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'<\/span>{0:2d}<\/span> {1:3d}<\/span> {2:4d}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>*<\/span>x<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>*<\/span>x<\/span>*<\/span>x<\/span>))<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n 1   1    1<\/span>\n 2   4    8<\/span>\n 3   9   27<\/span>\n 4  16   64<\/span>\n 5  25  125<\/span>\n 6  36  216<\/span>\n 7  49  343<\/span>\n 8  64  512<\/span>\n 9  81  729<\/span>\n10 100 1000<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    For a complete overview of string formatting with\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>, see\u00a0Format String Syntax<\/span>.<\/p><\/div>
                    7.1.3.\u00a0<\/span>Manual String Formatting<\/h3>
                    Here\u2019s the same table of squares and cubes, formatted manually:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>for<\/span> x<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 11<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>repr<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span>.<\/span>rjust<\/span>(<\/span>2<\/span>),<\/span> repr<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>*<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span>.<\/span>rjust<\/span>(<\/span>3<\/span>),<\/span> end<\/span>=<\/span>' '<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    # Note use of 'end' on previous line<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>repr<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>*<\/span>x<\/span>*<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span>.<\/span>rjust<\/span>(<\/span>4<\/span>))<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n 1   1    1<\/span>\n 2   4    8<\/span>\n 3   9   27<\/span>\n 4  16   64<\/span>\n 5  25  125<\/span>\n 6  36  216<\/span>\n 7  49  343<\/span>\n 8  64  512<\/span>\n 9  81  729<\/span>\n10 100 1000<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    (Note that the one space between each column was added by the way\u00a0print()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0works: it always adds spaces between its arguments.)<\/p>
                    The\u00a0str.rjust()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method of string objects right-justifies a string in a field of a given width by padding it with spaces on the left. There are similar methods\u00a0str.ljust()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0str.center()<\/span><\/code>. These methods do not write anything, they just return a new string. If the input string is too long, they don\u2019t truncate it, but return it unchanged; this will mess up your column lay-out but that\u2019s usually better than the alternative, which would be lying about a value. (If you really want truncation you can always add a slice operation, as in\u00a0x.ljust(n)[:n]<\/span><\/code>.)<\/p>
                    There is another method,\u00a0str.zfill()<\/span><\/code>, which pads a numeric string on the left with zeros. It understands about plus and minus signs:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>'12'<\/span>.<\/span>zfill<\/span>(<\/span>5<\/span>)<\/span>\n'00012'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>'-3.14'<\/span>.<\/span>zfill<\/span>(<\/span>7<\/span>)<\/span>\n'-003.14'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>'3.14159265359'<\/span>.<\/span>zfill<\/span>(<\/span>5<\/span>)<\/span>\n'3.14159265359'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                    7.1.4.\u00a0<\/span>Old string formatting<\/h3>
                    The % operator (modulo) can also be used for string formatting. Given\u00a0'string'<\/span>\u00a0%<\/span>\u00a0values<\/span><\/code>, instances of\u00a0%<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in\u00a0string<\/span><\/code>\u00a0are replaced with zero or more elements of\u00a0values<\/span><\/code>. This operation is commonly known as string interpolation. For example:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>import<\/span> math<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>'The value of pi is approximately <\/span>%5.3f<\/span>.'<\/span> %<\/span> math<\/span>.<\/span>pi<\/span>)<\/span>\nThe value of pi is approximately 3.142.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    More information can be found in the\u00a0printf-style String Formatting<\/span>\u00a0section.<\/p><\/div><\/div>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    7.2.\u00a0<\/span>Reading and Writing Files<\/h2>
                    open()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0returns a\u00a0file object<\/span>, and is most commonly used with two arguments:\u00a0open(filename,<\/span>\u00a0mode)<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>f<\/span> =<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>'workfile'<\/span>,<\/span> 'w'<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    The first argument is a string containing the filename. The second argument is another string containing a few characters describing the way in which the file will be used.\u00a0mode<\/em>\u00a0can be\u00a0'r'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0when the file will only be read,\u00a0'w'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0for only writing (an existing file with the same name will be erased), and\u00a0'a'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0opens the file for appending; any data written to the file is automatically added to the end.\u00a0'r+'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0opens the file for both reading and writing. The\u00a0mode<\/em>\u00a0argument is optional;\u00a0'r'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will be assumed if it\u2019s omitted.<\/p>
                    Normally, files are opened in\u00a0text mode<\/em>, that means, you read and write strings from and to the file, which are encoded in a specific encoding. If encoding is not specified, the default is platform dependent (see\u00a0open()<\/span><\/code>).\u00a0'b'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0appended to the mode opens the file in\u00a0binary mode<\/em>: now the data is read and written in the form of bytes objects. This mode should be used for all files that don\u2019t contain text.<\/p>
                    In text mode, the default when reading is to convert platform-specific line endings (\\n<\/span><\/code>\u00a0on Unix,\u00a0\\r\\n<\/span><\/code>\u00a0on Windows) to just\u00a0\\n<\/span><\/code>. When writing in text mode, the default is to convert occurrences of\u00a0\\n<\/span><\/code>\u00a0back to platform-specific line endings. This behind-the-scenes modification to file data is fine for text files, but will corrupt binary data like that in\u00a0JPEG<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0EXE<\/span><\/code>\u00a0files. Be very careful to use binary mode when reading and writing such files.<\/p>
                    It is good practice to use the\u00a0with<\/span><\/code>\u00a0keyword when dealing with file objects. The advantage is that the file is properly closed after its suite finishes, even if an exception is raised at some point. Using\u00a0with<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is also much shorter than writing equivalent\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>–finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0blocks:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>with<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>'workfile'<\/span>)<\/span> as<\/span> f<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    read_data<\/span> =<\/span> f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>()<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span># We can check that the file has been automatically closed.<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>closed<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    If you\u2019re not using the\u00a0with<\/span><\/code>\u00a0keyword, then you should call\u00a0f.close()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to close the file and immediately free up any system resources used by it.<\/p>
                    Warning<\/p>
                    Calling\u00a0f.write()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0without using the\u00a0with<\/span><\/code>\u00a0keyword or calling\u00a0f.close()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0might<\/strong>\u00a0result in the arguments of\u00a0f.write()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0not being completely written to the disk, even if the program exits successfully.<\/p><\/div>
                    After a file object is closed, either by a\u00a0with<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement or by calling\u00a0f.close()<\/span><\/code>, attempts to use the file object will automatically fail.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>close<\/span>()<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>()<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \nValueError<\/span>: I\/O operation on closed file.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    7.2.1.\u00a0<\/span>Methods of File Objects<\/h3>
                    The rest of the examples in this section will assume that a file object called\u00a0f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0has already been created.<\/p>
                    To read a file\u2019s contents, call\u00a0f.read(size)<\/span><\/code>, which reads some quantity of data and returns it as a string (in text mode) or bytes object (in binary mode).\u00a0size<\/em>\u00a0is an optional numeric argument. When\u00a0size<\/em>\u00a0is omitted or negative, the entire contents of the file will be read and returned; it\u2019s your problem if the file is twice as large as your machine\u2019s memory. Otherwise, at most\u00a0size<\/em>\u00a0characters (in text mode) or\u00a0size<\/em>\u00a0bytes (in binary mode) are read and returned. If the end of the file has been reached,\u00a0f.read()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will return an empty string (''<\/span><\/code>).<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>()<\/span>\n'This is the entire file.\\n'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>()<\/span>\n''<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    f.readline()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0reads a single line from the file; a newline character (\\n<\/span><\/code>) is left at the end of the string, and is only omitted on the last line of the file if the file doesn\u2019t end in a newline. This makes the return value unambiguous; if\u00a0f.readline()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0returns an empty string, the end of the file has been reached, while a blank line is represented by\u00a0'\\n'<\/span><\/code>, a string containing only a single newline.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>readline<\/span>()<\/span>\n'This is the first line of the file.\\n'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>readline<\/span>()<\/span>\n'Second line of the file\\n'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>readline<\/span>()<\/span>\n''<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    For reading lines from a file, you can loop over the file object. This is memory efficient, fast, and leads to simple code:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>for<\/span> line<\/span> in<\/span> f<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>line<\/span>,<\/span> end<\/span>=<\/span>''<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nThis is the first line of the file.<\/span>\nSecond line of the file<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    If you want to read all the lines of a file in a list you can also use\u00a0list(f)<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0f.readlines()<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                    f.write(string)<\/span><\/code>\u00a0writes the contents of\u00a0string<\/em>\u00a0to the file, returning the number of characters written.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>write<\/span>(<\/span>'This is a test<\/span>\\n<\/span>'<\/span>)<\/span>\n15<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    Other types of objects need to be converted \u2013 either to a string (in text mode) or a bytes object (in binary mode) \u2013 before writing them:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>value<\/span> =<\/span> (<\/span>'the answer'<\/span>,<\/span> 42<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>s<\/span> =<\/span> str<\/span>(<\/span>value<\/span>)<\/span>  # convert the tuple to string<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>write<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n18<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    f.tell()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0returns an integer giving the file object\u2019s current position in the file represented as number of bytes from the beginning of the file when in binary mode and an opaque number when in text mode.<\/p>
                    To change the file object\u2019s position, use\u00a0f.seek(offset,<\/span>\u00a0whence)<\/span><\/code>. The position is computed from adding\u00a0offset<\/em>\u00a0to a reference point; the reference point is selected by the\u00a0whence<\/em>\u00a0argument. A\u00a0whence<\/em>\u00a0value of 0 measures from the beginning of the file, 1 uses the current file position, and 2 uses the end of the file as the reference point.\u00a0whence<\/em>\u00a0can be omitted and defaults to 0, using the beginning of the file as the reference point.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>f<\/span> =<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>'workfile'<\/span>,<\/span> 'rb+'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>write<\/span>(<\/span>b<\/span>'0123456789abcdef'<\/span>)<\/span>\n16<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>seek<\/span>(<\/span>5<\/span>)<\/span>      # Go to the 6th byte in the file<\/span>\n5<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>(<\/span>1<\/span>)<\/span>\nb'5'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>seek<\/span>(<\/span>-<\/span>3<\/span>,<\/span> 2<\/span>)<\/span>  # Go to the 3rd byte before the end<\/span>\n13<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>(<\/span>1<\/span>)<\/span>\nb'd'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    In text files (those opened without a\u00a0b<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in the mode string), only seeks relative to the beginning of the file are allowed (the exception being seeking to the very file end with\u00a0seek(0,<\/span>\u00a02)<\/span><\/code>) and the only valid\u00a0offset<\/em>\u00a0values are those returned from the\u00a0f.tell()<\/span><\/code>, or zero. Any other\u00a0offset<\/em>\u00a0value produces undefined behaviour.<\/p>
                    File objects have some additional methods, such as\u00a0isatty()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0truncate()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0which are less frequently used; consult the Library Reference for a complete guide to file objects.<\/p><\/div>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    7.2.2.\u00a0<\/span>Saving structured data with\u00a0json<\/span><\/code><\/h3>
                    Strings can easily be written to and read from a file. Numbers take a bit more effort, since the\u00a0read()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method only returns strings, which will have to be passed to a function like\u00a0int()<\/span><\/code>, which takes a string like\u00a0'123'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and returns its numeric value 123. When you want to save more complex data types like nested lists and dictionaries, parsing and serializing by hand becomes complicated.<\/p>
                    Rather than having users constantly writing and debugging code to save complicated data types to files, Python allows you to use the popular data interchange format called\u00a0JSON (JavaScript Object Notation). The standard module called\u00a0json<\/span><\/code>\u00a0can take Python data hierarchies, and convert them to string representations; this process is called\u00a0serializing<\/em>. Reconstructing the data from the string representation is called\u00a0deserializing<\/em>. Between serializing and deserializing, the string representing the object may have been stored in a file or data, or sent over a network connection to some distant machine.<\/p>
                    Note<\/p>
                    The JSON format is commonly used by modern applications to allow for data exchange. Many programmers are already familiar with it, which makes it a good choice for interoperability.<\/p><\/div>
                    If you have an object\u00a0x<\/span><\/code>, you can view its JSON string representation with a simple line of code:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>import<\/span> json<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>json<\/span>.<\/span>dumps<\/span>([<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 'simple'<\/span>,<\/span> 'list'<\/span>])<\/span>\n'[1, \"simple\", \"list\"]'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    Another variant of the\u00a0dumps()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function, called\u00a0dump()<\/span><\/code>, simply serializes the object to a\u00a0text file<\/span>. So if\u00a0f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a\u00a0text file<\/span>\u00a0object opened for writing, we can do this:<\/p>
                    json<\/span>.<\/span>dump<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>,<\/span> f<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    To decode the object again, if\u00a0f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a\u00a0text file<\/span>\u00a0object which has been opened for reading:<\/p>
                    x<\/span> =<\/span> json<\/span>.<\/span>load<\/span>(<\/span>f<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    This simple serialization technique can handle lists and dictionaries, but serializing arbitrary class instances in JSON requires a bit of extra effort. The reference for the\u00a0json<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module contains an explanation of this.<\/p>
                    See also<\/p>
                    pickle<\/span><\/code>\u00a0– the pickle module<\/p>
                    Contrary to\u00a0JSON<\/span>,\u00a0pickle<\/em>\u00a0is a protocol which allows the serialization of arbitrarily complex Python objects. As such, it is specific to Python and cannot be used to communicate with applications written in other languages. It is also insecure by default: deserializing pickle data coming from an untrusted source can execute arbitrary code, if the data was crafted by a skilled attacker.<\/p><\/div><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                    8.\u00a0<\/span>Errors and Exceptions<\/h2>
                    Until now error messages haven\u2019t been more than mentioned, but if you have tried out the examples you have probably seen some. There are (at least) two distinguishable kinds of errors:\u00a0syntax errors<\/em>\u00a0and\u00a0exceptions<\/em>.<\/p>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    8.1.\u00a0<\/span>Syntax Errors<\/h2>
                    Syntax errors, also known as parsing errors, are perhaps the most common kind of complaint you get while you are still learning Python:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>while<\/span> True<\/span> print<\/span>(<\/span>'Hello world'<\/span>)<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>\n    while<\/span> True<\/span> print<\/span>(<\/span>'Hello world'<\/span>)<\/span>\n                   ^<\/span>\nSyntaxError<\/span>: invalid syntax<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    The parser repeats the offending line and displays a little \u2018arrow\u2019 pointing at the earliest point in the line where the error was detected. The error is caused by (or at least detected at) the token\u00a0preceding<\/em>\u00a0the arrow: in the example, the error is detected at the function\u00a0print()<\/span><\/code>, since a colon (':'<\/span><\/code>) is missing before it. File name and line number are printed so you know where to look in case the input came from a script.<\/p><\/div>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    8.2.\u00a0<\/span>Exceptions<\/h2>
                    Even if a statement or expression is syntactically correct, it may cause an error when an attempt is made to execute it. Errors detected during execution are called\u00a0exceptions<\/em>\u00a0and are not unconditionally fatal: you will soon learn how to handle them in Python programs. Most exceptions are not handled by programs, however, and result in error messages as shown here:<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>10<\/span> *<\/span> (<\/span>1<\/span>\/<\/span>0<\/span>)<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \nZeroDivisionError<\/span>: division by zero<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>4<\/span> +<\/span> spam<\/span>*<\/span>3<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \nNameError<\/span>: name 'spam' is not defined<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>'2'<\/span> +<\/span> 2<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \nTypeError<\/span>: Can't convert 'int' object to str implicitly<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    The last line of the error message indicates what happened. Exceptions come in different types, and the type is printed as part of the message: the types in the example are\u00a0ZeroDivisionError<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0NameError<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0TypeError<\/span><\/code>. The string printed as the exception type is the name of the built-in exception that occurred. This is true for all built-in exceptions, but need not be true for user-defined exceptions (although it is a useful convention). Standard exception names are built-in identifiers (not reserved keywords).<\/p>
                    The rest of the line provides detail based on the type of exception and what caused it.<\/p>
                    The preceding part of the error message shows the context where the exception occurred, in the form of a stack traceback. In general it contains a stack traceback listing source lines; however, it will not display lines read from standard input.<\/p>
                    Built-in Exceptions<\/span>\u00a0lists the built-in exceptions and their meanings.<\/p><\/div>
                    \u00a0<\/p>
                    8.3.\u00a0<\/span>Handling Exceptions<\/h2>
                    It is possible to write programs that handle selected exceptions. Look at the following example, which asks the user for input until a valid integer has been entered, but allows the user to interrupt the program (using\u00a0Control-C<\/kbd>\u00a0or whatever the operating system supports); note that a user-generated interruption is signalled by raising the\u00a0KeyboardInterrupt<\/span><\/code>\u00a0exception.<\/p>
                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                    >>> <\/span>while<\/span> True<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        x<\/span> =<\/span> int<\/span>(<\/span>input<\/span>(<\/span>\"Please enter a number: \"<\/span>))<\/span>\n... <\/span>        break<\/span>\n... <\/span>    except<\/span> ValueError<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"Oops!  That was no valid number.  Try again...\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                    The\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement works as follows.<\/p>
                    • First, the\u00a0try clause<\/em>\u00a0(the statement(s) between the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0keywords) is executed.<\/p><\/li>
                    • If no exception occurs, the\u00a0except clause<\/em>\u00a0is skipped and execution of the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement is finished.<\/p><\/li>
                    • If an exception occurs during execution of the try clause, the rest of the clause is skipped. Then if its type matches the exception named after the\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0keyword, the except clause is executed, and then execution continues after the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement.<\/p><\/li>
                    • If an exception occurs which does not match the exception named in the except clause, it is passed on to outer\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statements; if no handler is found, it is an\u00a0unhandled exception<\/em>\u00a0and execution stops with a message as shown above.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                      A\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement may have more than one except clause, to specify handlers for different exceptions. At most one handler will be executed. Handlers only handle exceptions that occur in the corresponding try clause, not in other handlers of the same\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement. An except clause may name multiple exceptions as a parenthesized tuple, for example:<\/p>
                      ...<\/span> except<\/span> (<\/span>RuntimeError<\/span>,<\/span> TypeError<\/span>,<\/span> NameError<\/span>):<\/span>\n...<\/span>     pass<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      A class in an\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause is compatible with an exception if it is the same class or a base class thereof (but not the other way around \u2014 an except clause listing a derived class is not compatible with a base class). For example, the following code will print B, C, D in that order:<\/p>
                      class<\/span> B<\/span>(<\/span>Exception<\/span>):<\/span>\n    pass<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> C<\/span>(<\/span>B<\/span>):<\/span>\n    pass<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> D<\/span>(<\/span>C<\/span>):<\/span>\n    pass<\/span>\n\nfor<\/span> cls<\/span> in<\/span> [<\/span>B<\/span>,<\/span> C<\/span>,<\/span> D<\/span>]:<\/span>\n    try<\/span>:<\/span>\n        raise<\/span> cls<\/span>()<\/span>\n    except<\/span> D<\/span>:<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"D\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n    except<\/span> C<\/span>:<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"C\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n    except<\/span> B<\/span>:<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"B\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      Note that if the except clauses were reversed (with\u00a0except<\/span>\u00a0B<\/span><\/code>\u00a0first), it would have printed B, B, B \u2014 the first matching except clause is triggered.<\/p>
                      The last except clause may omit the exception name(s), to serve as a wildcard. Use this with extreme caution, since it is easy to mask a real programming error in this way! It can also be used to print an error message and then re-raise the exception (allowing a caller to handle the exception as well):<\/p>
                      import<\/span> sys<\/span>\n\ntry<\/span>:<\/span>\n    f<\/span> =<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>'myfile.txt'<\/span>)<\/span>\n    s<\/span> =<\/span> f<\/span>.<\/span>readline<\/span>()<\/span>\n    i<\/span> =<\/span> int<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>.<\/span>strip<\/span>())<\/span>\nexcept<\/span> OSError<\/span> as<\/span> err<\/span>:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>\"OS error: <\/span>{0}<\/span>\"<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>err<\/span>))<\/span>\nexcept<\/span> ValueError<\/span>:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>\"Could not convert data to an integer.\"<\/span>)<\/span>\nexcept<\/span>:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>\"Unexpected error:\"<\/span>,<\/span> sys<\/span>.<\/span>exc_info<\/span>()[<\/span>0<\/span>])<\/span>\n    raise<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      The\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0\u2026\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement has an optional\u00a0else clause<\/em>, which, when present, must follow all except clauses. It is useful for code that must be executed if the try clause does not raise an exception. For example:<\/p>
                      for<\/span> arg<\/span> in<\/span> sys<\/span>.<\/span>argv<\/span>[<\/span>1<\/span>:]:<\/span>\n    try<\/span>:<\/span>\n        f<\/span> =<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>arg<\/span>,<\/span> 'r'<\/span>)<\/span>\n    except<\/span> OSError<\/span>:<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>'cannot open'<\/span>,<\/span> arg<\/span>)<\/span>\n    else<\/span>:<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>arg<\/span>,<\/span> 'has'<\/span>,<\/span> len<\/span>(<\/span>f<\/span>.<\/span>readlines<\/span>()),<\/span> 'lines'<\/span>)<\/span>\n        f<\/span>.<\/span>close<\/span>()<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      The use of the\u00a0else<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause is better than adding additional code to the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause because it avoids accidentally catching an exception that wasn\u2019t raised by the code being protected by the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0\u2026\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement.<\/p>
                      When an exception occurs, it may have an associated value, also known as the exception\u2019s\u00a0argument<\/em>. The presence and type of the argument depend on the exception type.<\/p>
                      The except clause may specify a variable after the exception name. The variable is bound to an exception instance with the arguments stored in\u00a0instance.args<\/span><\/code>. For convenience, the exception instance defines\u00a0__str__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0so the arguments can be printed directly without having to reference\u00a0.args<\/span><\/code>. One may also instantiate an exception first before raising it and add any attributes to it as desired.<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span> Exception<\/span>(<\/span>'spam'<\/span>,<\/span> 'eggs'<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>except<\/span> Exception<\/span> as<\/span> inst<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>type<\/span>(<\/span>inst<\/span>))<\/span>    # the exception instance<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>inst<\/span>.<\/span>args<\/span>)<\/span>     # arguments stored in .args<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>inst<\/span>)<\/span>          # __str__ allows args to be printed directly,<\/span>\n... <\/span>                         # but may be overridden in exception subclasses<\/span>\n... <\/span>    x<\/span>,<\/span> y<\/span> =<\/span> inst<\/span>.<\/span>args<\/span>     # unpack args<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'x ='<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'y ='<\/span>,<\/span> y<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n<class 'Exception'><\/span>\n('spam', 'eggs')<\/span>\n('spam', 'eggs')<\/span>\nx = spam<\/span>\ny = eggs<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      If an exception has arguments, they are printed as the last part (\u2018detail\u2019) of the message for unhandled exceptions.<\/p>
                      Exception handlers don\u2019t just handle exceptions if they occur immediately in the try clause, but also if they occur inside functions that are called (even indirectly) in the try clause. For example:<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>def<\/span> this_fails<\/span>():<\/span>\n... <\/span>    x<\/span> =<\/span> 1<\/span>\/<\/span>0<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    this_fails<\/span>()<\/span>\n... <\/span>except<\/span> ZeroDivisionError<\/span> as<\/span> err<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'Handling run-time error:'<\/span>,<\/span> err<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nHandling run-time error: division by zero<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                      \u00a0<\/p>
                      8.4.\u00a0<\/span>Raising Exceptions<\/h2>
                      The\u00a0raise<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement allows the programmer to force a specified exception to occur. For example:<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>raise<\/span> NameError<\/span>(<\/span>'HiThere'<\/span>)<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \nNameError<\/span>: HiThere<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      The sole argument to\u00a0raise<\/span><\/code>\u00a0indicates the exception to be raised. This must be either an exception instance or an exception class (a class that derives from\u00a0Exception<\/span><\/code>). If an exception class is passed, it will be implicitly instantiated by calling its constructor with no arguments:<\/p>
                      raise<\/span> ValueError<\/span>  # shorthand for 'raise ValueError()'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      If you need to determine whether an exception was raised but don\u2019t intend to handle it, a simpler form of the\u00a0raise<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement allows you to re-raise the exception:<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span> NameError<\/span>(<\/span>'HiThere'<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>except<\/span> NameError<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'An exception flew by!'<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nAn exception flew by!<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 2<\/span>, in \nNameError<\/span>: HiThere<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                      \u00a0<\/p>
                      8.5.\u00a0<\/span>Exception Chaining<\/h2>
                      The\u00a0raise<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement allows an optional\u00a0from<\/span><\/code>\u00a0which enables chaining exceptions. For example:<\/p>
                      # exc must be exception instance or None.<\/span>\nraise<\/span> RuntimeError<\/span> from<\/span> exc<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      This can be useful when you are transforming exceptions. For example:<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>def<\/span> func<\/span>():<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span> IOError<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    func<\/span>()<\/span>\n... <\/span>except<\/span> IOError<\/span> as<\/span> exc<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span> RuntimeError<\/span>(<\/span>'Failed to open database'<\/span>)<\/span> from<\/span> exc<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 2<\/span>, in \n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 2<\/span>, in func<\/span>\nOSError<\/span>\n\nThe above exception was the direct cause of the following exception:<\/span>\n\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 4<\/span>, in \nRuntimeError<\/span>: Failed to open database<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      Exception chaining happens automatically when an exception is raised inside an\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0section. Exception chaining can be disabled by using\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0None<\/span><\/code>\u00a0idiom:<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    open<\/span>(<\/span>'database.sqlite'<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>except<\/span> IOError<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span> RuntimeError<\/span> from<\/span> None<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 4<\/span>, in \nRuntimeError<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      For more information about chaining mechanics, see\u00a0Built-in Exceptions<\/span>.<\/p><\/div>
                      \u00a0<\/p>
                      8.6.\u00a0<\/span>User-defined Exceptions<\/h2>
                      Programs may name their own exceptions by creating a new exception class (see\u00a0Classes<\/span>\u00a0for more about Python classes). Exceptions should typically be derived from the\u00a0Exception<\/span><\/code>\u00a0class, either directly or indirectly.<\/p>
                      Exception classes can be defined which do anything any other class can do, but are usually kept simple, often only offering a number of attributes that allow information about the error to be extracted by handlers for the exception. When creating a module that can raise several distinct errors, a common practice is to create a base class for exceptions defined by that module, and subclass that to create specific exception classes for different error conditions:<\/p>
                      class<\/span> Error<\/span>(<\/span>Exception<\/span>):<\/span>\n    \"\"\"Base class for exceptions in this module.\"\"\"<\/span>\n    pass<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> InputError<\/span>(<\/span>Error<\/span>):<\/span>\n    \"\"\"Exception raised for errors in the input.<\/span>\n\n    Attributes:<\/span>\n        expression -- input expression in which the error occurred<\/span>\n        message -- explanation of the error<\/span>\n    \"\"\"<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> expression<\/span>,<\/span> message<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>expression<\/span> =<\/span> expression<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>message<\/span> =<\/span> message<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> TransitionError<\/span>(<\/span>Error<\/span>):<\/span>\n    \"\"\"Raised when an operation attempts a state transition that's not<\/span>\n    allowed.<\/span>\n\n    Attributes:<\/span>\n        previous -- state at beginning of transition<\/span>\n        next -- attempted new state<\/span>\n        message -- explanation of why the specific transition is not allowed<\/span>\n    \"\"\"<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> previous<\/span>,<\/span> next<\/span>,<\/span> message<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>previous<\/span> =<\/span> previous<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>next<\/span> =<\/span> next<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>message<\/span> =<\/span> message<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      Most exceptions are defined with names that end in \u201cError\u201d, similar to the naming of the standard exceptions.<\/p>
                      Many standard modules define their own exceptions to report errors that may occur in functions they define. More information on classes is presented in chapter\u00a0Classes<\/span>.<\/p><\/div>
                      \u00a0<\/p>
                      8.7.\u00a0<\/span>Defining Clean-up Actions<\/h2>
                      The\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement has another optional clause which is intended to define clean-up actions that must be executed under all circumstances. For example:<\/p>
                      >>><\/span><\/p>
                      >>> <\/span>try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    raise<\/span> KeyboardInterrupt<\/span>\n... <\/span>finally<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'Goodbye, world!'<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nGoodbye, world!<\/span>\nKeyboardInterrupt<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 2<\/span>, in \n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                      If a\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause is present, the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause will execute as the last task before the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement completes. The\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause runs whether or not the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement produces an exception. The following points discuss more complex cases when an exception occurs:<\/p>
                      • If an exception occurs during execution of the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause, the exception may be handled by an\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause. If the exception is not handled by an\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause, the exception is re-raised after the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause has been executed.<\/p><\/li>
                      • An exception could occur during execution of an\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0else<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause. Again, the exception is re-raised after the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause has been executed.<\/p><\/li>
                      • If the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement reaches a\u00a0break<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0continue<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0return<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement, the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause will execute just prior to the\u00a0break<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0continue<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0return<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement\u2019s execution.<\/p><\/li>
                      • If a\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause includes a\u00a0return<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement, the returned value will be the one from the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause\u2019s\u00a0return<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement, not the value from the\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause\u2019s\u00a0return<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                        For example:<\/p>
                        >>><\/span><\/p>
                        >>> <\/span>def<\/span> bool_return<\/span>():<\/span>\n... <\/span>    try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        return<\/span> True<\/span>\n... <\/span>    finally<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        return<\/span> False<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>bool_return<\/span>()<\/span>\nFalse<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                        A more complicated example:<\/p>
                        >>><\/span><\/p>
                        >>> <\/span>def<\/span> divide<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>,<\/span> y<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>    try<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        result<\/span> =<\/span> x<\/span> \/<\/span> y<\/span>\n... <\/span>    except<\/span> ZeroDivisionError<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"division by zero!\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    else<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"result is\"<\/span>,<\/span> result<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    finally<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        print<\/span>(<\/span>\"executing finally clause\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>divide<\/span>(<\/span>2<\/span>,<\/span> 1<\/span>)<\/span>\nresult is 2.0<\/span>\nexecuting finally clause<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>divide<\/span>(<\/span>2<\/span>,<\/span> 0<\/span>)<\/span>\ndivision by zero!<\/span>\nexecuting finally clause<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>divide<\/span>(<\/span>\"2\"<\/span>,<\/span> \"1\"<\/span>)<\/span>\nexecuting finally clause<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 3<\/span>, in divide<\/span>\nTypeError<\/span>: unsupported operand type(s) for \/: 'str' and 'str'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                        As you can see, the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause is executed in any event. The\u00a0TypeError<\/span><\/code>\u00a0raised by dividing two strings is not handled by the\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause and therefore re-raised after the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause has been executed.<\/p>
                        In real world applications, the\u00a0finally<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause is useful for releasing external resources (such as files or network connections), regardless of whether the use of the resource was successful.<\/p><\/div>
                        \u00a0<\/p>
                        8.8.\u00a0<\/span>Predefined Clean-up Actions<\/h2>
                        Some objects define standard clean-up actions to be undertaken when the object is no longer needed, regardless of whether or not the operation using the object succeeded or failed. Look at the following example, which tries to open a file and print its contents to the screen.<\/p>
                        for<\/span> line<\/span> in<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>\"myfile.txt\"<\/span>):<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>line<\/span>,<\/span> end<\/span>=<\/span>\"\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                        The problem with this code is that it leaves the file open for an indeterminate amount of time after this part of the code has finished executing. This is not an issue in simple scripts, but can be a problem for larger applications. The\u00a0with<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement allows objects like files to be used in a way that ensures they are always cleaned up promptly and correctly.<\/p>
                        with<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>\"myfile.txt\"<\/span>)<\/span> as<\/span> f<\/span>:<\/span>\n    for<\/span> line<\/span> in<\/span> f<\/span>:<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>line<\/span>,<\/span> end<\/span>=<\/span>\"\"<\/span>)<\/span><\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                        \n\t\t\t\t\t
                        9.\u00a0<\/span>Classes<\/h2>
                        Classes provide a means of bundling data and functionality together. Creating a new class creates a new\u00a0type<\/em>\u00a0of object, allowing new\u00a0instances<\/em>\u00a0of that type to be made. Each class instance can have attributes attached to it for maintaining its state. Class instances can also have methods (defined by its class) for modifying its state.<\/p>
                        Compared with other programming languages, Python\u2019s class mechanism adds classes with a minimum of new syntax and semantics. It is a mixture of the class mechanisms found in C++ and Modula-3. Python classes provide all the standard features of Object Oriented Programming: the class inheritance mechanism allows multiple base classes, a derived class can override any methods of its base class or classes, and a method can call the method of a base class with the same name. Objects can contain arbitrary amounts and kinds of data. As is true for modules, classes partake of the dynamic nature of Python: they are created at runtime, and can be modified further after creation.<\/p>
                        In C++ terminology, normally class members (including the data members) are\u00a0public<\/em>\u00a0(except see below\u00a0Private Variables<\/span>), and all member functions are\u00a0virtual<\/em>. As in Modula-3, there are no shorthands for referencing the object\u2019s members from its methods: the method function is declared with an explicit first argument representing the object, which is provided implicitly by the call. As in Smalltalk, classes themselves are objects. This provides semantics for importing and renaming. Unlike C++ and Modula-3, built-in types can be used as base classes for extension by the user. Also, like in C++, most built-in operators with special syntax (arithmetic operators, subscripting etc.) can be redefined for class instances.<\/p>
                        (Lacking universally accepted terminology to talk about classes, I will make occasional use of Smalltalk and C++ terms. I would use Modula-3 terms, since its object-oriented semantics are closer to those of Python than C++, but I expect that few readers have heard of it.)<\/p>
                        \u00a0<\/p>
                        9.1.\u00a0<\/span>A Word About Names and Objects<\/h2>
                        Objects have individuality, and multiple names (in multiple scopes) can be bound to the same object. This is known as aliasing in other languages. This is usually not appreciated on a first glance at Python, and can be safely ignored when dealing with immutable basic types (numbers, strings, tuples). However, aliasing has a possibly surprising effect on the semantics of Python code involving mutable objects such as lists, dictionaries, and most other types. This is usually used to the benefit of the program, since aliases behave like pointers in some respects. For example, passing an object is cheap since only a pointer is passed by the implementation; and if a function modifies an object passed as an argument, the caller will see the change \u2014 this eliminates the need for two different argument passing mechanisms as in Pascal.<\/p><\/div>
                        \u00a0<\/p>
                        9.2.\u00a0<\/span>Python Scopes and Namespaces<\/h2>
                        Before introducing classes, I first have to tell you something about Python\u2019s scope rules. Class definitions play some neat tricks with namespaces, and you need to know how scopes and namespaces work to fully understand what\u2019s going on. Incidentally, knowledge about this subject is useful for any advanced Python programmer.<\/p>
                        Let\u2019s begin with some definitions.<\/p>
                        A\u00a0namespace<\/em>\u00a0is a mapping from names to objects. Most namespaces are currently implemented as Python dictionaries, but that\u2019s normally not noticeable in any way (except for performance), and it may change in the future. Examples of namespaces are: the set of built-in names (containing functions such as\u00a0abs()<\/span><\/code>, and built-in exception names); the global names in a module; and the local names in a function invocation. In a sense the set of attributes of an object also form a namespace. The important thing to know about namespaces is that there is absolutely no relation between names in different namespaces; for instance, two different modules may both define a function\u00a0maximize<\/span><\/code>\u00a0without confusion \u2014 users of the modules must prefix it with the module name.<\/p>
                        By the way, I use the word\u00a0attribute<\/em>\u00a0for any name following a dot \u2014 for example, in the expression\u00a0z.real<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0real<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is an attribute of the object\u00a0z<\/span><\/code>. Strictly speaking, references to names in modules are attribute references: in the expression\u00a0modname.funcname<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0modname<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a module object and\u00a0funcname<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is an attribute of it. In this case there happens to be a straightforward mapping between the module\u2019s attributes and the global names defined in the module: they share the same namespace!\u00a01<\/p>
                        Attributes may be read-only or writable. In the latter case, assignment to attributes is possible. Module attributes are writable: you can write\u00a0modname.the_answer<\/span>\u00a0=<\/span>\u00a042<\/span><\/code>. Writable attributes may also be deleted with the\u00a0del<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement. For example,\u00a0del<\/span>\u00a0modname.the_answer<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will remove the attribute\u00a0the_answer<\/span><\/code>\u00a0from the object named by\u00a0modname<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                        Namespaces are created at different moments and have different lifetimes. The namespace containing the built-in names is created when the Python interpreter starts up, and is never deleted. The global namespace for a module is created when the module definition is read in; normally, module namespaces also last until the interpreter quits. The statements executed by the top-level invocation of the interpreter, either read from a script file or interactively, are considered part of a module called\u00a0__main__<\/span><\/code>, so they have their own global namespace. (The built-in names actually also live in a module; this is called\u00a0builtins<\/span><\/code>.)<\/p>
                        The local namespace for a function is created when the function is called, and deleted when the function returns or raises an exception that is not handled within the function. (Actually, forgetting would be a better way to describe what actually happens.) Of course, recursive invocations each have their own local namespace.<\/p>
                        A\u00a0scope<\/em>\u00a0is a textual region of a Python program where a namespace is directly accessible. \u201cDirectly accessible\u201d here means that an unqualified reference to a name attempts to find the name in the namespace.<\/p>
                        Although scopes are determined statically, they are used dynamically. At any time during execution, there are 3 or 4 nested scopes whose namespaces are directly accessible:<\/p>
                        • the innermost scope, which is searched first, contains the local names<\/p><\/li>
                        • the scopes of any enclosing functions, which are searched starting with the nearest enclosing scope, contains non-local, but also non-global names<\/p><\/li>
                        • the next-to-last scope contains the current module\u2019s global names<\/p><\/li>
                        • the outermost scope (searched last) is the namespace containing built-in names<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                          If a name is declared global, then all references and assignments go directly to the middle scope containing the module\u2019s global names. To rebind variables found outside of the innermost scope, the\u00a0nonlocal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement can be used; if not declared nonlocal, those variables are read-only (an attempt to write to such a variable will simply create a\u00a0new<\/em>\u00a0local variable in the innermost scope, leaving the identically named outer variable unchanged).<\/p>
                          Usually, the local scope references the local names of the (textually) current function. Outside functions, the local scope references the same namespace as the global scope: the module\u2019s namespace. Class definitions place yet another namespace in the local scope.<\/p>
                          It is important to realize that scopes are determined textually: the global scope of a function defined in a module is that module\u2019s namespace, no matter from where or by what alias the function is called. On the other hand, the actual search for names is done dynamically, at run time \u2014 however, the language definition is evolving towards static name resolution, at \u201ccompile\u201d time, so don\u2019t rely on dynamic name resolution! (In fact, local variables are already determined statically.)<\/p>
                          A special quirk of Python is that \u2013 if no\u00a0global<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0nonlocal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement is in effect \u2013 assignments to names always go into the innermost scope. Assignments do not copy data \u2014 they just bind names to objects. The same is true for deletions: the statement\u00a0del<\/span>\u00a0x<\/span><\/code>\u00a0removes the binding of\u00a0x<\/span><\/code>\u00a0from the namespace referenced by the local scope. In fact, all operations that introduce new names use the local scope: in particular,\u00a0import<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statements and function definitions bind the module or function name in the local scope.<\/p>
                          The\u00a0global<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement can be used to indicate that particular variables live in the global scope and should be rebound there; the\u00a0nonlocal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement indicates that particular variables live in an enclosing scope and should be rebound there.<\/p>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.2.1.\u00a0<\/span>Scopes and Namespaces Example<\/h3>
                          This is an example demonstrating how to reference the different scopes and namespaces, and how\u00a0global<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0nonlocal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0affect variable binding:<\/p>
                          def<\/span> scope_test<\/span>():<\/span>\n    def<\/span> do_local<\/span>():<\/span>\n        spam<\/span> =<\/span> \"local spam\"<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> do_nonlocal<\/span>():<\/span>\n        nonlocal<\/span> spam<\/span>\n        spam<\/span> =<\/span> \"nonlocal spam\"<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> do_global<\/span>():<\/span>\n        global<\/span> spam<\/span>\n        spam<\/span> =<\/span> \"global spam\"<\/span>\n\n    spam<\/span> =<\/span> \"test spam\"<\/span>\n    do_local<\/span>()<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>\"After local assignment:\"<\/span>,<\/span> spam<\/span>)<\/span>\n    do_nonlocal<\/span>()<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>\"After nonlocal assignment:\"<\/span>,<\/span> spam<\/span>)<\/span>\n    do_global<\/span>()<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>\"After global assignment:\"<\/span>,<\/span> spam<\/span>)<\/span>\n\nscope_test<\/span>()<\/span>\nprint<\/span>(<\/span>\"In global scope:\"<\/span>,<\/span> spam<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          The output of the example code is:<\/p>
                          After local assignment: test spam\nAfter nonlocal assignment: nonlocal spam\nAfter global assignment: nonlocal spam\nIn global scope: global spam\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Note how the\u00a0local<\/em>\u00a0assignment (which is default) didn\u2019t change\u00a0scope_test<\/em>\u2019s binding of\u00a0spam<\/em>. The\u00a0nonlocal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0assignment changed\u00a0scope_test<\/em>\u2019s binding of\u00a0spam<\/em>, and the\u00a0global<\/span><\/code>\u00a0assignment changed the module-level binding.<\/p>
                          You can also see that there was no previous binding for\u00a0spam<\/em>\u00a0before the\u00a0global<\/span><\/code>\u00a0assignment.<\/p><\/div><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.3.\u00a0<\/span>A First Look at Classes<\/h2>
                          Classes introduce a little bit of new syntax, three new object types, and some new semantics.<\/p>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.3.1.\u00a0<\/span>Class Definition Syntax<\/h3>
                          The simplest form of class definition looks like this:<\/p>
