                          class<\/span> ClassName<\/span>:<\/span>\n    <<\/span>statement<\/span>-<\/span>1<\/span>><\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    <<\/span>statement<\/span>-<\/span>N<\/span>><\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Class definitions, like function definitions (def<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statements) must be executed before they have any effect. (You could conceivably place a class definition in a branch of an\u00a0if<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement, or inside a function.)<\/p>
                          In practice, the statements inside a class definition will usually be function definitions, but other statements are allowed, and sometimes useful \u2014 we\u2019ll come back to this later. The function definitions inside a class normally have a peculiar form of argument list, dictated by the calling conventions for methods \u2014 again, this is explained later.<\/p>
                          When a class definition is entered, a new namespace is created, and used as the local scope \u2014 thus, all assignments to local variables go into this new namespace. In particular, function definitions bind the name of the new function here.<\/p>
                          When a class definition is left normally (via the end), a\u00a0class object<\/em>\u00a0is created. This is basically a wrapper around the contents of the namespace created by the class definition; we\u2019ll learn more about class objects in the next section. The original local scope (the one in effect just before the class definition was entered) is reinstated, and the class object is bound here to the class name given in the class definition header (ClassName<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in the example).<\/p><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.3.2.\u00a0<\/span>Class Objects<\/h3>
                          Class objects support two kinds of operations: attribute references and instantiation.<\/p>
                          Attribute references<\/em>\u00a0use the standard syntax used for all attribute references in Python:\u00a0obj.name<\/span><\/code>. Valid attribute names are all the names that were in the class\u2019s namespace when the class object was created. So, if the class definition looked like this:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> MyClass<\/span>:<\/span>\n    \"\"\"A simple example class\"\"\"<\/span>\n    i<\/span> =<\/span> 12345<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> f<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        return<\/span> 'hello world'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          then\u00a0MyClass.i<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0MyClass.f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0are valid attribute references, returning an integer and a function object, respectively. Class attributes can also be assigned to, so you can change the value of\u00a0MyClass.i<\/span><\/code>\u00a0by assignment.\u00a0__doc__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is also a valid attribute, returning the docstring belonging to the class:\u00a0\"A<\/span>\u00a0simple<\/span>\u00a0example<\/span>\u00a0class\"<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                          Class\u00a0instantiation<\/em>\u00a0uses function notation. Just pretend that the class object is a parameterless function that returns a new instance of the class. For example (assuming the above class):<\/p>
                          x<\/span> =<\/span> MyClass<\/span>()<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          creates a new\u00a0instance<\/em>\u00a0of the class and assigns this object to the local variable\u00a0x<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                          The instantiation operation (\u201ccalling\u201d a class object) creates an empty object. Many classes like to create objects with instances customized to a specific initial state. Therefore a class may define a special method named\u00a0__init__()<\/span><\/code>, like this:<\/p>
                          def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n    self<\/span>.<\/span>data<\/span> =<\/span> []<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          When a class defines an\u00a0__init__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method, class instantiation automatically invokes\u00a0__init__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0for the newly-created class instance. So in this example, a new, initialized instance can be obtained by:<\/p>
                          x<\/span> =<\/span> MyClass<\/span>()<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Of course, the\u00a0__init__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method may have arguments for greater flexibility. In that case, arguments given to the class instantiation operator are passed on to\u00a0__init__()<\/span><\/code>. For example,<\/p>
                          >>><\/span><\/p>
                          >>> <\/span>class<\/span> Complex<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> realpart<\/span>,<\/span> imagpart<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>        self<\/span>.<\/span>r<\/span> =<\/span> realpart<\/span>\n... <\/span>        self<\/span>.<\/span>i<\/span> =<\/span> imagpart<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>x<\/span> =<\/span> Complex<\/span>(<\/span>3.0<\/span>,<\/span> -<\/span>4.5<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>x<\/span>.<\/span>r<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>.<\/span>i<\/span>\n(3.0, -4.5)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.3.3.\u00a0<\/span>Instance Objects<\/h3>
                          Now what can we do with instance objects? The only operations understood by instance objects are attribute references. There are two kinds of valid attribute names: data attributes and methods.<\/p>
                          data attributes<\/em>\u00a0correspond to \u201cinstance variables\u201d in Smalltalk, and to \u201cdata members\u201d in C++. Data attributes need not be declared; like local variables, they spring into existence when they are first assigned to. For example, if\u00a0x<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is the instance of\u00a0MyClass<\/span><\/code>\u00a0created above, the following piece of code will print the value\u00a016<\/span><\/code>, without leaving a trace:<\/p>
                          x<\/span>.<\/span>counter<\/span> =<\/span> 1<\/span>\nwhile<\/span> x<\/span>.<\/span>counter<\/span> <<\/span> 10<\/span>:<\/span>\n    x<\/span>.<\/span>counter<\/span> =<\/span> x<\/span>.<\/span>counter<\/span> *<\/span> 2<\/span>\nprint<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>.<\/span>counter<\/span>)<\/span>\ndel<\/span> x<\/span>.<\/span>counter<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          The other kind of instance attribute reference is a\u00a0method<\/em>. A method is a function that \u201cbelongs to\u201d an object. (In Python, the term method is not unique to class instances: other object types can have methods as well. For example, list objects have methods called append, insert, remove, sort, and so on. However, in the following discussion, we\u2019ll use the term method exclusively to mean methods of class instance objects, unless explicitly stated otherwise.)<\/p>
                          Valid method names of an instance object depend on its class. By definition, all attributes of a class that are function objects define corresponding methods of its instances. So in our example,\u00a0x.f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a valid method reference, since\u00a0MyClass.f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a function, but\u00a0x.i<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is not, since\u00a0MyClass.i<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is not. But\u00a0x.f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is not the same thing as\u00a0MyClass.f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0\u2014 it is a\u00a0method object<\/em>, not a function object.<\/p><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.3.4.\u00a0<\/span>Method Objects<\/h3>
                          Usually, a method is called right after it is bound:<\/p>
                          x<\/span>.<\/span>f<\/span>()<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          In the\u00a0MyClass<\/span><\/code>\u00a0example, this will return the string\u00a0'hello<\/span>\u00a0world'<\/span><\/code>. However, it is not necessary to call a method right away:\u00a0x.f<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a method object, and can be stored away and called at a later time. For example:<\/p>
                          xf<\/span> =<\/span> x<\/span>.<\/span>f<\/span>\nwhile<\/span> True<\/span>:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>xf<\/span>())<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          will continue to print\u00a0hello<\/span>\u00a0world<\/span><\/code>\u00a0until the end of time.<\/p>
                          What exactly happens when a method is called? You may have noticed that\u00a0x.f()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0was called without an argument above, even though the function definition for\u00a0f()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0specified an argument. What happened to the argument? Surely Python raises an exception when a function that requires an argument is called without any \u2014 even if the argument isn\u2019t actually used\u2026<\/p>
                          Actually, you may have guessed the answer: the special thing about methods is that the instance object is passed as the first argument of the function. In our example, the call\u00a0x.f()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is exactly equivalent to\u00a0MyClass.f(x)<\/span><\/code>. In general, calling a method with a list of\u00a0n<\/em>\u00a0arguments is equivalent to calling the corresponding function with an argument list that is created by inserting the method\u2019s instance object before the first argument.<\/p>
                          If you still don\u2019t understand how methods work, a look at the implementation can perhaps clarify matters. When a non-data attribute of an instance is referenced, the instance\u2019s class is searched. If the name denotes a valid class attribute that is a function object, a method object is created by packing (pointers to) the instance object and the function object just found together in an abstract object: this is the method object. When the method object is called with an argument list, a new argument list is constructed from the instance object and the argument list, and the function object is called with this new argument list.<\/p><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.3.5.\u00a0<\/span>Class and Instance Variables<\/h3>
                          Generally speaking, instance variables are for data unique to each instance and class variables are for attributes and methods shared by all instances of the class:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> Dog<\/span>:<\/span>\n\n    kind<\/span> =<\/span> 'canine'<\/span>         # class variable shared by all instances<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> name<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span> =<\/span> name<\/span>    # instance variable unique to each instance<\/span>\n\n>>><\/span> d<\/span> =<\/span> Dog<\/span>(<\/span>'Fido'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span> =<\/span> Dog<\/span>(<\/span>'Buddy'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>kind<\/span>                  # shared by all dogs<\/span>\n'canine'<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span>.<\/span>kind<\/span>                  # shared by all dogs<\/span>\n'canine'<\/span>\n>>><\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span>                  # unique to d<\/span>\n'Fido'<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span>                  # unique to e<\/span>\n'Buddy'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          As discussed in\u00a0A Word About Names and Objects<\/span>, shared data can have possibly surprising effects with involving\u00a0mutable<\/span>\u00a0objects such as lists and dictionaries. For example, the\u00a0tricks<\/em>\u00a0list in the following code should not be used as a class variable because just a single list would be shared by all\u00a0Dog<\/em>\u00a0instances:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> Dog<\/span>:<\/span>\n\n    tricks<\/span> =<\/span> []<\/span>             # mistaken use of a class variable<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> name<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span> =<\/span> name<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> add_trick<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> trick<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>tricks<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>trick<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n>>><\/span> d<\/span> =<\/span> Dog<\/span>(<\/span>'Fido'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span> =<\/span> Dog<\/span>(<\/span>'Buddy'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>add_trick<\/span>(<\/span>'roll over'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span>.<\/span>add_trick<\/span>(<\/span>'play dead'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>tricks<\/span>                # unexpectedly shared by all dogs<\/span>\n[<\/span>'roll over'<\/span>,<\/span> 'play dead'<\/span>]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Correct design of the class should use an instance variable instead:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> Dog<\/span>:<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> name<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span> =<\/span> name<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>tricks<\/span> =<\/span> []<\/span>    # creates a new empty list for each dog<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> add_trick<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> trick<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>tricks<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>trick<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n>>><\/span> d<\/span> =<\/span> Dog<\/span>(<\/span>'Fido'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span> =<\/span> Dog<\/span>(<\/span>'Buddy'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>add_trick<\/span>(<\/span>'roll over'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span>.<\/span>add_trick<\/span>(<\/span>'play dead'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>><\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>tricks<\/span>\n[<\/span>'roll over'<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>><\/span> e<\/span>.<\/span>tricks<\/span>\n[<\/span>'play dead'<\/span>]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.4.\u00a0<\/span>Random Remarks<\/h2>
                          If the same attribute name occurs in both an instance and in a class, then attribute lookup prioritizes the instance:<\/p>
                          >>><\/span><\/p>
                          >>> <\/span>class<\/span> Warehouse<\/span>:<\/span>\n        purpose = 'storage'<\/span>\n        region = 'west'<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>w1<\/span> =<\/span> Warehouse<\/span>()<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>w1<\/span>.<\/span>purpose<\/span>,<\/span> w1<\/span>.<\/span>region<\/span>)<\/span>\nstorage west<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>w2<\/span> =<\/span> Warehouse<\/span>()<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>w2<\/span>.<\/span>region<\/span> =<\/span> 'east'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>w2<\/span>.<\/span>purpose<\/span>,<\/span> w2<\/span>.<\/span>region<\/span>)<\/span>\nstorage east<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Data attributes may be referenced by methods as well as by ordinary users (\u201cclients\u201d) of an object. In other words, classes are not usable to implement pure abstract data types. In fact, nothing in Python makes it possible to enforce data hiding \u2014 it is all based upon convention. (On the other hand, the Python implementation, written in C, can completely hide implementation details and control access to an object if necessary; this can be used by extensions to Python written in C.)<\/p>
                          Clients should use data attributes with care \u2014 clients may mess up invariants maintained by the methods by stamping on their data attributes. Note that clients may add data attributes of their own to an instance object without affecting the validity of the methods, as long as name conflicts are avoided \u2014 again, a naming convention can save a lot of headaches here.<\/p>
                          There is no shorthand for referencing data attributes (or other methods!) from within methods. I find that this actually increases the readability of methods: there is no chance of confusing local variables and instance variables when glancing through a method.<\/p>
                          Often, the first argument of a method is called\u00a0self<\/span><\/code>. This is nothing more than a convention: the name\u00a0self<\/span><\/code>\u00a0has absolutely no special meaning to Python. Note, however, that by not following the convention your code may be less readable to other Python programmers, and it is also conceivable that a\u00a0class browser<\/em>\u00a0program might be written that relies upon such a convention.<\/p>
                          Any function object that is a class attribute defines a method for instances of that class. It is not necessary that the function definition is textually enclosed in the class definition: assigning a function object to a local variable in the class is also ok. For example:<\/p>
                          # Function defined outside the class<\/span>\ndef<\/span> f1<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>,<\/span> y<\/span>):<\/span>\n    return<\/span> min<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>+<\/span>y<\/span>)<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> C<\/span>:<\/span>\n    f<\/span> =<\/span> f1<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> g<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        return<\/span> 'hello world'<\/span>\n\n    h<\/span> =<\/span> g<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Now\u00a0f<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0g<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0h<\/span><\/code>\u00a0are all attributes of class\u00a0C<\/span><\/code>\u00a0that refer to function objects, and consequently they are all methods of instances of\u00a0C<\/span><\/code>\u00a0\u2014\u00a0h<\/span><\/code>\u00a0being exactly equivalent to\u00a0g<\/span><\/code>. Note that this practice usually only serves to confuse the reader of a program.<\/p>
                          Methods may call other methods by using method attributes of the\u00a0self<\/span><\/code>\u00a0argument:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> Bag<\/span>:<\/span>\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>data<\/span> =<\/span> []<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> add<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>data<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> addtwice<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>add<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>add<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Methods may reference global names in the same way as ordinary functions. The global scope associated with a method is the module containing its definition. (A class is never used as a global scope.) While one rarely encounters a good reason for using global data in a method, there are many legitimate uses of the global scope: for one thing, functions and modules imported into the global scope can be used by methods, as well as functions and classes defined in it. Usually, the class containing the method is itself defined in this global scope, and in the next section we\u2019ll find some good reasons why a method would want to reference its own class.<\/p>
                          Each value is an object, and therefore has a\u00a0class<\/em>\u00a0(also called its\u00a0type<\/em>). It is stored as\u00a0object.__class__<\/span><\/code>.<\/p><\/div>
                          \u00a0<\/p>
                          9.5.\u00a0<\/span>Inheritance<\/h2>
                          Of course, a language feature would not be worthy of the name \u201cclass\u201d without supporting inheritance. The syntax for a derived class definition looks like this:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> DerivedClassName<\/span>(<\/span>BaseClassName<\/span>):<\/span>\n    <<\/span>statement<\/span>-<\/span>1<\/span>><\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    <<\/span>statement<\/span>-<\/span>N<\/span>><\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          The name\u00a0BaseClassName<\/span><\/code>\u00a0must be defined in a scope containing the derived class definition. In place of a base class name, other arbitrary expressions are also allowed. This can be useful, for example, when the base class is defined in another module:<\/p>
                          class<\/span> DerivedClassName<\/span>(<\/span>modname<\/span>.<\/span>BaseClassName<\/span>):<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                          Execution of a derived class definition proceeds the same as for a base class. When the class object is constructed, the base class is remembered. This is used for resolving attribute references: if a requested attribute is not found in the class, the search proceeds to look in the base class. This rule is applied recursively if the base class itself is derived from some other class.<\/p>
                          There\u2019s nothing special about instantiation of derived classes:\u00a0DerivedClassName()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0creates a new instance of the class. Method references are resolved as follows: the corresponding class attribute is searched, descending down the chain of base classes if necessary, and the method reference is valid if this yields a function object.<\/p>
                          Derived classes may override methods of their base classes. Because methods have no special privileges when calling other methods of the same object, a method of a base class that calls another method defined in the same base class may end up calling a method of a derived class that overrides it. (For C++ programmers: all methods in Python are effectively\u00a0virtual<\/span><\/code>.)<\/p>
                          An overriding method in a derived class may in fact want to extend rather than simply replace the base class method of the same name. There is a simple way to call the base class method directly: just call\u00a0BaseClassName.methodname(self,<\/span>\u00a0arguments)<\/span><\/code>. This is occasionally useful to clients as well. (Note that this only works if the base class is accessible as\u00a0BaseClassName<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in the global scope.)<\/p>
                          Python has two built-in functions that work with inheritance:<\/p>
                          • Use\u00a0isinstance()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to check an instance\u2019s type:\u00a0isinstance(obj,<\/span>\u00a0int)<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will be\u00a0True<\/span><\/code>\u00a0only if\u00a0obj.__class__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is\u00a0int<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or some class derived from\u00a0int<\/span><\/code>.<\/p><\/li>
                          • Use\u00a0issubclass()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to check class inheritance:\u00a0issubclass(bool,<\/span>\u00a0int)<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is\u00a0True<\/span><\/code>\u00a0since\u00a0bool<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is a subclass of\u00a0int<\/span><\/code>. However,\u00a0issubclass(float,<\/span>\u00a0int)<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is\u00a0False<\/span><\/code>\u00a0since\u00a0float<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is not a subclass of\u00a0int<\/span><\/code>.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            9.5.1.\u00a0<\/span>Multiple Inheritance<\/h3>
                            Python supports a form of multiple inheritance as well. A class definition with multiple base classes looks like this:<\/p>
                            class<\/span> DerivedClassName<\/span>(<\/span>Base1<\/span>,<\/span> Base2<\/span>,<\/span> Base3<\/span>):<\/span>\n    <<\/span>statement<\/span>-<\/span>1<\/span>><\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    .<\/span>\n    <<\/span>statement<\/span>-<\/span>N<\/span>><\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            For most purposes, in the simplest cases, you can think of the search for attributes inherited from a parent class as depth-first, left-to-right, not searching twice in the same class where there is an overlap in the hierarchy. Thus, if an attribute is not found in\u00a0DerivedClassName<\/span><\/code>, it is searched for in\u00a0Base1<\/span><\/code>, then (recursively) in the base classes of\u00a0Base1<\/span><\/code>, and if it was not found there, it was searched for in\u00a0Base2<\/span><\/code>, and so on.<\/p>
                            In fact, it is slightly more complex than that; the method resolution order changes dynamically to support cooperative calls to\u00a0super()<\/span><\/code>. This approach is known in some other multiple-inheritance languages as call-next-method and is more powerful than the super call found in single-inheritance languages.<\/p>
                            Dynamic ordering is necessary because all cases of multiple inheritance exhibit one or more diamond relationships (where at least one of the parent classes can be accessed through multiple paths from the bottommost class). For example, all classes inherit from\u00a0object<\/span><\/code>, so any case of multiple inheritance provides more than one path to reach\u00a0object<\/span><\/code>. To keep the base classes from being accessed more than once, the dynamic algorithm linearizes the search order in a way that preserves the left-to-right ordering specified in each class, that calls each parent only once, and that is monotonic (meaning that a class can be subclassed without affecting the precedence order of its parents). Taken together, these properties make it possible to design reliable and extensible classes with multiple inheritance. For more detail, see\u00a0https:\/\/www.python.org\/download\/releases\/2.3\/mro\/.<\/p><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            9.6.\u00a0<\/span>Private Variables<\/h2>
                            \u201cPrivate\u201d instance variables that cannot be accessed except from inside an object don\u2019t exist in Python. However, there is a convention that is followed by most Python code: a name prefixed with an underscore (e.g.\u00a0_spam<\/span><\/code>) should be treated as a non-public part of the API (whether it is a function, a method or a data member). It should be considered an implementation detail and subject to change without notice.<\/p>
                            Since there is a valid use-case for class-private members (namely to avoid name clashes of names with names defined by subclasses), there is limited support for such a mechanism, called\u00a0name mangling<\/em>. Any identifier of the form\u00a0__spam<\/span><\/code>\u00a0(at least two leading underscores, at most one trailing underscore) is textually replaced with\u00a0_classname__spam<\/span><\/code>, where\u00a0classname<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is the current class name with leading underscore(s) stripped. This mangling is done without regard to the syntactic position of the identifier, as long as it occurs within the definition of a class.<\/p>
                            Name mangling is helpful for letting subclasses override methods without breaking intraclass method calls. For example:<\/p>
                            class<\/span> Mapping<\/span>:<\/span>\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> iterable<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>items_list<\/span> =<\/span> []<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>__update<\/span>(<\/span>iterable<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> update<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> iterable<\/span>):<\/span>\n        for<\/span> item<\/span> in<\/span> iterable<\/span>:<\/span>\n            self<\/span>.<\/span>items_list<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>item<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n    __update<\/span> =<\/span> update<\/span>   # private copy of original update() method<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> MappingSubclass<\/span>(<\/span>Mapping<\/span>):<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> update<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> keys<\/span>,<\/span> values<\/span>):<\/span>\n        # provides new signature for update()<\/span>\n        # but does not break __init__()<\/span>\n        for<\/span> item<\/span> in<\/span> zip<\/span>(<\/span>keys<\/span>,<\/span> values<\/span>):<\/span>\n            self<\/span>.<\/span>items_list<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>item<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The above example would work even if\u00a0MappingSubclass<\/span><\/code>\u00a0were to introduce a\u00a0__update<\/span><\/code>\u00a0identifier since it is replaced with\u00a0_Mapping__update<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in the\u00a0Mapping<\/span><\/code>\u00a0class and\u00a0_MappingSubclass__update<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in the\u00a0MappingSubclass<\/span><\/code>\u00a0class respectively.<\/p>
                            Note that the mangling rules are designed mostly to avoid accidents; it still is possible to access or modify a variable that is considered private. This can even be useful in special circumstances, such as in the debugger.<\/p>
                            Notice that code passed to\u00a0exec()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or\u00a0eval()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0does not consider the classname of the invoking class to be the current class; this is similar to the effect of the\u00a0global<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement, the effect of which is likewise restricted to code that is byte-compiled together. The same restriction applies to\u00a0getattr()<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0setattr()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0delattr()<\/span><\/code>, as well as when referencing\u00a0__dict__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0directly.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            9.7.\u00a0<\/span>Odds and Ends<\/h2>
                            Sometimes it is useful to have a data type similar to the Pascal \u201crecord\u201d or C \u201cstruct\u201d, bundling together a few named data items. An empty class definition will do nicely:<\/p>
                            class<\/span> Employee<\/span>:<\/span>\n    pass<\/span>\n\njohn<\/span> =<\/span> Employee<\/span>()<\/span>  # Create an empty employee record<\/span>\n\n# Fill the fields of the record<\/span>\njohn<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span> =<\/span> 'John Doe'<\/span>\njohn<\/span>.<\/span>dept<\/span> =<\/span> 'computer lab'<\/span>\njohn<\/span>.<\/span>salary<\/span> =<\/span> 1000<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            A piece of Python code that expects a particular abstract data type can often be passed a class that emulates the methods of that data type instead. For instance, if you have a function that formats some data from a file object, you can define a class with methods\u00a0read()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0readline()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0that get the data from a string buffer instead, and pass it as an argument.<\/p>
                            Instance method objects have attributes, too:\u00a0m.__self__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is the instance object with the method\u00a0m()<\/span><\/code>, and\u00a0m.__func__<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is the function object corresponding to the method.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            9.8.\u00a0<\/span>Iterators<\/h2>
                            By now you have probably noticed that most container objects can be looped over using a\u00a0for<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement:<\/p>
                            for<\/span> element<\/span> in<\/span> [<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 2<\/span>,<\/span> 3<\/span>]:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>element<\/span>)<\/span>\nfor<\/span> element<\/span> in<\/span> (<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 2<\/span>,<\/span> 3<\/span>):<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>element<\/span>)<\/span>\nfor<\/span> key<\/span> in<\/span> {<\/span>'one'<\/span>:<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 'two'<\/span>:<\/span>2<\/span>}:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>key<\/span>)<\/span>\nfor<\/span> char<\/span> in<\/span> \"123\"<\/span>:<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>char<\/span>)<\/span>\nfor<\/span> line<\/span> in<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>\"myfile.txt\"<\/span>):<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>line<\/span>,<\/span> end<\/span>=<\/span>''<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            This style of access is clear, concise, and convenient. The use of iterators pervades and unifies Python. Behind the scenes, the\u00a0for<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement calls\u00a0iter()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0on the container object. The function returns an iterator object that defines the method\u00a0__next__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0which accesses elements in the container one at a time. When there are no more elements,\u00a0__next__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0raises a\u00a0StopIteration<\/span><\/code>\u00a0exception which tells the\u00a0for<\/span><\/code>\u00a0loop to terminate. You can call the\u00a0__next__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method using the\u00a0next()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0built-in function; this example shows how it all works:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>s<\/span> =<\/span> 'abc'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>it<\/span> =<\/span> iter<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>it<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>next<\/span>(<\/span>it<\/span>)<\/span>\n'a'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>next<\/span>(<\/span>it<\/span>)<\/span>\n'b'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>next<\/span>(<\/span>it<\/span>)<\/span>\n'c'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>next<\/span>(<\/span>it<\/span>)<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \n    next<\/span>(<\/span>it<\/span>)<\/span>\nStopIteration<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            Having seen the mechanics behind the iterator protocol, it is easy to add iterator behavior to your classes. Define an\u00a0__iter__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method which returns an object with a\u00a0__next__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method. If the class defines\u00a0__next__()<\/span><\/code>, then\u00a0__iter__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0can just return\u00a0self<\/span><\/code>:<\/p>
                            class<\/span> Reverse<\/span>:<\/span>\n    \"\"\"Iterator for looping over a sequence backwards.\"\"\"<\/span>\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> data<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>data<\/span> =<\/span> data<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>index<\/span> =<\/span> len<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __iter__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        return<\/span> self<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> __next__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        if<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>index<\/span> ==<\/span> 0<\/span>:<\/span>\n            raise<\/span> StopIteration<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>index<\/span> =<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>index<\/span> -<\/span> 1<\/span>\n        return<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>data<\/span>[<\/span>self<\/span>.<\/span>index<\/span>]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>rev<\/span> =<\/span> Reverse<\/span>(<\/span>'spam'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>iter<\/span>(<\/span>rev<\/span>)<\/span>\n<__main__.Reverse object at 0x00A1DB50><\/span>\n>>> <\/span>for<\/span> char<\/span> in<\/span> rev<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>char<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nm<\/span>\na<\/span>\np<\/span>\ns<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            9.9.\u00a0<\/span>Generators<\/h2>
                            Generators<\/span>\u00a0are a simple and powerful tool for creating iterators. They are written like regular functions but use the\u00a0yield<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement whenever they want to return data. Each time\u00a0next()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is called on it, the generator resumes where it left off (it remembers all the data values and which statement was last executed). An example shows that generators can be trivially easy to create:<\/p>
                            def<\/span> reverse<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>):<\/span>\n    for<\/span> index<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>len<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>-<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> -<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> -<\/span>1<\/span>):<\/span>\n        yield<\/span> data<\/span>[<\/span>index<\/span>]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>for<\/span> char<\/span> in<\/span> reverse<\/span>(<\/span>'golf'<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>char<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\nf<\/span>\nl<\/span>\no<\/span>\ng<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            Anything that can be done with generators can also be done with class-based iterators as described in the previous section. What makes generators so compact is that the\u00a0__iter__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0__next__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0methods are created automatically.<\/p>
                            Another key feature is that the local variables and execution state are automatically saved between calls. This made the function easier to write and much more clear than an approach using instance variables like\u00a0self.index<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0self.data<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                            In addition to automatic method creation and saving program state, when generators terminate, they automatically raise\u00a0StopIteration<\/span><\/code>. In combination, these features make it easy to create iterators with no more effort than writing a regular function.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            9.10.\u00a0<\/span>Generator Expressions<\/h2>
                            Some simple generators can be coded succinctly as expressions using a syntax similar to list comprehensions but with parentheses instead of square brackets. These expressions are designed for situations where the generator is used right away by an enclosing function. Generator expressions are more compact but less versatile than full generator definitions and tend to be more memory friendly than equivalent list comprehensions.<\/p>
                            Examples:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>sum<\/span>(<\/span>i<\/span>*<\/span>i<\/span> for<\/span> i<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>10<\/span>))<\/span>                 # sum of squares<\/span>\n285<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>xvec<\/span> =<\/span> [<\/span>10<\/span>,<\/span> 20<\/span>,<\/span> 30<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>yvec<\/span> =<\/span> [<\/span>7<\/span>,<\/span> 5<\/span>,<\/span> 3<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>sum<\/span>(<\/span>x<\/span>*<\/span>y<\/span> for<\/span> x<\/span>,<\/span>y<\/span> in<\/span> zip<\/span>(<\/span>xvec<\/span>,<\/span> yvec<\/span>))<\/span>         # dot product<\/span>\n260<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>unique_words<\/span> =<\/span> set<\/span>(<\/span>word<\/span> for<\/span> line<\/span> in<\/span> page<\/span>  for<\/span> word<\/span> in<\/span> line<\/span>.<\/span>split<\/span>())<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>valedictorian<\/span> =<\/span> max<\/span>((<\/span>student<\/span>.<\/span>gpa<\/span>,<\/span> student<\/span>.<\/span>name<\/span>)<\/span> for<\/span> student<\/span> in<\/span> graduates<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>data<\/span> =<\/span> 'golf'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>list<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>[<\/span>i<\/span>]<\/span> for<\/span> i<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>len<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>-<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> -<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> -<\/span>1<\/span>))<\/span>\n['f', 'l', 'o', 'g']<\/span><\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                            \n\t\t\t\t\t
                            10.\u00a0<\/span>Brief Tour of the Standard Library<\/h2>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.1.\u00a0<\/span>Operating System Interface<\/h2>
                            The\u00a0os<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides dozens of functions for interacting with the operating system:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> os<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>os<\/span>.<\/span>getcwd<\/span>()<\/span>      # Return the current working directory<\/span>\n'C:\\\\Python39'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>os<\/span>.<\/span>chdir<\/span>(<\/span>'\/server\/accesslogs'<\/span>)<\/span>   # Change current working directory<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>os<\/span>.<\/span>system<\/span>(<\/span>'mkdir today'<\/span>)<\/span>   # Run the command mkdir in the system shell<\/span>\n0<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            Be sure to use the\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0os<\/span><\/code>\u00a0style instead of\u00a0from<\/span>\u00a0os<\/span>\u00a0import<\/span>\u00a0*<\/span><\/code>. This will keep\u00a0os.open()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0from shadowing the built-in\u00a0open()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function which operates much differently.<\/p>
                            The built-in\u00a0dir()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0help()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0functions are useful as interactive aids for working with large modules like\u00a0os<\/span><\/code>:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> os<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>dir<\/span>(<\/span>os<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>help<\/span>(<\/span>os<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            For daily file and directory management tasks, the\u00a0shutil<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides a higher level interface that is easier to use:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> shutil<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>shutil<\/span>.<\/span>copyfile<\/span>(<\/span>'data.db'<\/span>,<\/span> 'archive.db'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'archive.db'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>shutil<\/span>.<\/span>move<\/span>(<\/span>'\/build\/executables'<\/span>,<\/span> 'installdir'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'installdir'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.2.\u00a0<\/span>File Wildcards<\/h2>
                            The\u00a0glob<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides a function for making file lists from directory wildcard searches:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> glob<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>glob<\/span>.<\/span>glob<\/span>(<\/span>'*.py'<\/span>)<\/span>\n['primes.py', 'random.py', 'quote.py']<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.3.\u00a0<\/span>Command Line Arguments<\/h2>
                            Common utility scripts often need to process command line arguments. These arguments are stored in the\u00a0sys<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module\u2019s\u00a0argv<\/em>\u00a0attribute as a list. For instance the following output results from running\u00a0python<\/span>\u00a0demo.py<\/span>\u00a0one<\/span>\u00a0two<\/span>\u00a0three<\/span><\/code>\u00a0at the command line:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> sys<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>sys<\/span>.<\/span>argv<\/span>)<\/span>\n['demo.py', 'one', 'two', 'three']<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The\u00a0argparse<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides a more sophisticated mechanism to process command line arguments. The following script extracts one or more filenames and an optional number of lines to be displayed:<\/p>
                            import<\/span> argparse<\/span>\n\nparser<\/span> =<\/span> argparse<\/span>.<\/span>ArgumentParser<\/span>(<\/span>prog<\/span> =<\/span> 'top'<\/span>,<\/span>\n    description<\/span> =<\/span> 'Show top lines from each file'<\/span>)<\/span>\nparser<\/span>.<\/span>add_argument<\/span>(<\/span>'filenames'<\/span>,<\/span> nargs<\/span>=<\/span>'+'<\/span>)<\/span>\nparser<\/span>.<\/span>add_argument<\/span>(<\/span>'-l'<\/span>,<\/span> '--lines'<\/span>,<\/span> type<\/span>=<\/span>int<\/span>,<\/span> default<\/span>=<\/span>10<\/span>)<\/span>\nargs<\/span> =<\/span> parser<\/span>.<\/span>parse_args<\/span>()<\/span>\nprint<\/span>(<\/span>args<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            When run at the command line with\u00a0python<\/span>\u00a0top.py<\/span>\u00a0--lines=5<\/span>\u00a0alpha.txt<\/span>\u00a0beta.txt<\/span><\/code>, the script sets\u00a0args.lines<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to\u00a05<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0args.filenames<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to\u00a0['alpha.txt',<\/span>\u00a0'beta.txt']<\/span><\/code>.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.4.\u00a0<\/span>Error Output Redirection and Program Termination<\/h2>
                            The\u00a0sys<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module also has attributes for\u00a0stdin<\/em>,\u00a0stdout<\/em>, and\u00a0stderr<\/em>. The latter is useful for emitting warnings and error messages to make them visible even when\u00a0stdout<\/em>\u00a0has been redirected:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>sys<\/span>.<\/span>stderr<\/span>.<\/span>write<\/span>(<\/span>'Warning, log file not found starting a new one<\/span>\\n<\/span>'<\/span>)<\/span>\nWarning, log file not found starting a new one<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The most direct way to terminate a script is to use\u00a0sys.exit()<\/span><\/code>.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.5.\u00a0<\/span>String Pattern Matching<\/h2>
                            The\u00a0re<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides regular expression tools for advanced string processing. For complex matching and manipulation, regular expressions offer succinct, optimized solutions:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> re<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>re<\/span>.<\/span>findall<\/span>(<\/span>r<\/span>'\\bf[a-z]*'<\/span>,<\/span> 'which foot or hand fell fastest'<\/span>)<\/span>\n['foot', 'fell', 'fastest']<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>re<\/span>.<\/span>sub<\/span>(<\/span>r<\/span>'(\\b[a-z]+) \\1'<\/span>,<\/span> r<\/span>'\\1'<\/span>,<\/span> 'cat in the the hat'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'cat in the hat'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            When only simple capabilities are needed, string methods are preferred because they are easier to read and debug:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>'tea for too'<\/span>.<\/span>replace<\/span>(<\/span>'too'<\/span>,<\/span> 'two'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'tea for two'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.6.\u00a0<\/span>Mathematics<\/h2>
                            The\u00a0math<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module gives access to the underlying C library functions for floating point math:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> math<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>cos<\/span>(<\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>pi<\/span> \/<\/span> 4<\/span>)<\/span>\n0.70710678118654757<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>log<\/span>(<\/span>1024<\/span>,<\/span> 2<\/span>)<\/span>\n10.0<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The\u00a0random<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides tools for making random selections:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> random<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>random<\/span>.<\/span>choice<\/span>([<\/span>'apple'<\/span>,<\/span> 'pear'<\/span>,<\/span> 'banana'<\/span>])<\/span>\n'apple'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>random<\/span>.<\/span>sample<\/span>(<\/span>range<\/span>(<\/span>100<\/span>),<\/span> 10<\/span>)<\/span>   # sampling without replacement<\/span>\n[30, 83, 16, 4, 8, 81, 41, 50, 18, 33]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>random<\/span>.<\/span>random<\/span>()<\/span>    # random float<\/span>\n0.17970987693706186<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>random<\/span>.<\/span>randrange<\/span>(<\/span>6<\/span>)<\/span>    # random integer chosen from range(6)<\/span>\n4<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The\u00a0statistics<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module calculates basic statistical properties (the mean, median, variance, etc.) of numeric data:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> statistics<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>data<\/span> =<\/span> [<\/span>2.75<\/span>,<\/span> 1.75<\/span>,<\/span> 1.25<\/span>,<\/span> 0.25<\/span>,<\/span> 0.5<\/span>,<\/span> 1.25<\/span>,<\/span> 3.5<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>statistics<\/span>.<\/span>mean<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>\n1.6071428571428572<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>statistics<\/span>.<\/span>median<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>\n1.25<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>statistics<\/span>.<\/span>variance<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>\n1.3720238095238095<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The SciPy project <https:\/\/scipy.org> has many other modules for numerical computations.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.7.\u00a0<\/span>Internet Access<\/h2>
                            There are a number of modules for accessing the internet and processing internet protocols. Two of the simplest are\u00a0urllib.request<\/span><\/code>\u00a0for retrieving data from URLs and\u00a0smtplib<\/span><\/code>\u00a0for sending mail:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>from<\/span> urllib.request<\/span> import<\/span> urlopen<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>with<\/span> urlopen<\/span>(<\/span>'http:\/\/tycho.usno.navy.mil\/cgi-bin\/timer.pl'<\/span>)<\/span> as<\/span> response<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    for<\/span> line<\/span> in<\/span> response<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>        line<\/span> =<\/span> line<\/span>.<\/span>decode<\/span>(<\/span>'utf-8'<\/span>)<\/span>  # Decoding the binary data to text.<\/span>\n... <\/span>        if<\/span> 'EST'<\/span> in<\/span> line<\/span> or<\/span> 'EDT'<\/span> in<\/span> line<\/span>:<\/span>  # look for Eastern Time<\/span>\n... <\/span>            print<\/span>(<\/span>line<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n
                            Nov. 25, 09:43:32 PM EST<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>import<\/span> smtplib<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>server<\/span> =<\/span> smtplib<\/span>.<\/span>SMTP<\/span>(<\/span>'localhost'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>server<\/span>.<\/span>sendmail<\/span>(<\/span>'soothsayer@example.org'<\/span>,<\/span> 'jcaesar@example.org'<\/span>,<\/span>\n... <\/span>\"\"\"To: jcaesar@example.org<\/span>\n... <\/span>From: soothsayer@example.org<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n... <\/span>Beware the Ides of March.<\/span>\n... <\/span>\"\"\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>server<\/span>.<\/span>quit<\/span>()<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            (Note that the second example needs a mailserver running on localhost.)<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.8.\u00a0<\/span>Dates and Times<\/h2>
                            The\u00a0datetime<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module supplies classes for manipulating dates and times in both simple and complex ways. While date and time arithmetic is supported, the focus of the implementation is on efficient member extraction for output formatting and manipulation. The module also supports objects that are timezone aware.<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span># dates are easily constructed and formatted<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>from<\/span> datetime<\/span> import<\/span> date<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>now<\/span> =<\/span> date<\/span>.<\/span>today<\/span>()<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>now<\/span>\ndatetime.date(2003, 12, 2)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>now<\/span>.<\/span>strftime<\/span>(<\/span>\"%m-<\/span>%d<\/span>-%y. <\/span>%d<\/span> %b %Y is a %A on the <\/span>%d<\/span> day of %B.\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n'12-02-03. 02 Dec 2003 is a Tuesday on the 02 day of December.'<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span># dates support calendar arithmetic<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>birthday<\/span> =<\/span> date<\/span>(<\/span>1964<\/span>,<\/span> 7<\/span>,<\/span> 31<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>age<\/span> =<\/span> now<\/span> -<\/span> birthday<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>age<\/span>.<\/span>days<\/span>\n14368<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.9.\u00a0<\/span>Data Compression<\/h2>
                            Common data archiving and compression formats are directly supported by modules including:\u00a0zlib<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0gzip<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0bz2<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0lzma<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0zipfile<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0tarfile<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>import<\/span> zlib<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>s<\/span> =<\/span> b<\/span>'witch which has which witches wrist watch'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>len<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n41<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span> =<\/span> zlib<\/span>.<\/span>compress<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>len<\/span>(<\/span>t<\/span>)<\/span>\n37<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>zlib<\/span>.<\/span>decompress<\/span>(<\/span>t<\/span>)<\/span>\nb'witch which has which witches wrist watch'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>zlib<\/span>.<\/span>crc32<\/span>(<\/span>s<\/span>)<\/span>\n226805979<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.10.\u00a0<\/span>Performance Measurement<\/h2>
                            Some Python users develop a deep interest in knowing the relative performance of different approaches to the same problem. Python provides a measurement tool that answers those questions immediately.<\/p>
                            For example, it may be tempting to use the tuple packing and unpacking feature instead of the traditional approach to swapping arguments. The\u00a0timeit<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module quickly demonstrates a modest performance advantage:<\/p>
                            >>><\/span><\/p>
                            >>> <\/span>from<\/span> timeit<\/span> import<\/span> Timer<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>Timer<\/span>(<\/span>'t=a; a=b; b=t'<\/span>,<\/span> 'a=1; b=2'<\/span>)<\/span>.<\/span>timeit<\/span>()<\/span>\n0.57535828626024577<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>Timer<\/span>(<\/span>'a,b = b,a'<\/span>,<\/span> 'a=1; b=2'<\/span>)<\/span>.<\/span>timeit<\/span>()<\/span>\n0.54962537085770791<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            In contrast to\u00a0timeit<\/span><\/code>\u2019s fine level of granularity, the\u00a0profile<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0pstats<\/span><\/code>\u00a0modules provide tools for identifying time critical sections in larger blocks of code.<\/p><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.11.\u00a0<\/span>Quality Control<\/h2>
                            One approach for developing high quality software is to write tests for each function as it is developed and to run those tests frequently during the development process.<\/p>
                            The\u00a0doctest<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides a tool for scanning a module and validating tests embedded in a program\u2019s docstrings. Test construction is as simple as cutting-and-pasting a typical call along with its results into the docstring. This improves the documentation by providing the user with an example and it allows the doctest module to make sure the code remains true to the documentation:<\/p>
                            def<\/span> average<\/span>(<\/span>values<\/span>):<\/span>\n    \"\"\"Computes the arithmetic mean of a list of numbers.<\/span>\n\n    >>> print(average([20, 30, 70]))<\/span>\n    40.0<\/span>\n    \"\"\"<\/span>\n    return<\/span> sum<\/span>(<\/span>values<\/span>)<\/span> \/<\/span> len<\/span>(<\/span>values<\/span>)<\/span>\n\nimport<\/span> doctest<\/span>\ndoctest<\/span>.<\/span>testmod<\/span>()<\/span>   # automatically validate the embedded tests<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                            The\u00a0unittest<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module is not as effortless as the\u00a0doctest<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module, but it allows a more comprehensive set of tests to be maintained in a separate file:<\/p>
                            import<\/span> unittest<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> TestStatisticalFunctions<\/span>(<\/span>unittest<\/span>.<\/span>TestCase<\/span>):<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> test_average<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>assertEqual<\/span>(<\/span>average<\/span>([<\/span>20<\/span>,<\/span> 30<\/span>,<\/span> 70<\/span>]),<\/span> 40.0<\/span>)<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>assertEqual<\/span>(<\/span>round<\/span>(<\/span>average<\/span>([<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 5<\/span>,<\/span> 7<\/span>]),<\/span> 1<\/span>),<\/span> 4.3<\/span>)<\/span>\n        with<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>assertRaises<\/span>(<\/span>ZeroDivisionError<\/span>):<\/span>\n            average<\/span>([])<\/span>\n        with<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>assertRaises<\/span>(<\/span>TypeError<\/span>):<\/span>\n            average<\/span>(<\/span>20<\/span>,<\/span> 30<\/span>,<\/span> 70<\/span>)<\/span>\n\nunittest<\/span>.<\/span>main<\/span>()<\/span>  # Calling from the command line invokes all tests<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                            \u00a0<\/p>
                            10.12.\u00a0<\/span>Batteries Included<\/h2>
                            Python has a \u201cbatteries included\u201d philosophy. This is best seen through the sophisticated and robust capabilities of its larger packages. For example:<\/p>
                            • The\u00a0xmlrpc.client<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0xmlrpc.server<\/span><\/code>\u00a0modules make implementing remote procedure calls into an almost trivial task. Despite the modules names, no direct knowledge or handling of XML is needed.<\/p><\/li>
                            • The\u00a0email<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package is a library for managing email messages, including MIME and other\u00a0<\/span>RFC 2822<\/strong>-based message documents. Unlike\u00a0smtplib<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0poplib<\/span><\/code>\u00a0which actually send and receive messages, the email package has a complete toolset for building or decoding complex message structures (including attachments) and for implementing internet encoding and header protocols.<\/p><\/li>
                            • The\u00a0json<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package provides robust support for parsing this popular data interchange format. The\u00a0csv<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module supports direct reading and writing of files in Comma-Separated Value format, commonly supported by databases and spreadsheets. XML processing is supported by the\u00a0xml.etree.ElementTree<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0xml.dom<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0xml.sax<\/span><\/code>\u00a0packages. Together, these modules and packages greatly simplify data interchange between Python applications and other tools.<\/p><\/li>
                            • The\u00a0sqlite3<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module is a wrapper for the SQLite database library, providing a persistent database that can be updated and accessed using slightly nonstandard SQL syntax.<\/p><\/li>
                            • Internationalization is supported by a number of modules including\u00a0gettext<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0locale<\/span><\/code>, and the\u00a0codecs<\/span><\/code>\u00a0package.<\/p><\/li><\/ul><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                              \n\t\t\t\t\t
                              11.\u00a0<\/span>Brief Tour of the Standard Library \u2014 Part II<\/h2>
                              This second tour covers more advanced modules that support professional programming needs. These modules rarely occur in small scripts.<\/p>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.1.\u00a0<\/span>Output Formatting<\/h2>
                              The\u00a0reprlib<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides a version of\u00a0repr()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0customized for abbreviated displays of large or deeply nested containers:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> reprlib<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>reprlib<\/span>.<\/span>repr<\/span>(<\/span>set<\/span>(<\/span>'supercalifragilisticexpialidocious'<\/span>))<\/span>\n\"{'a', 'c', 'd', 'e', 'f', 'g', ...}\"<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The\u00a0pprint<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module offers more sophisticated control over printing both built-in and user defined objects in a way that is readable by the interpreter. When the result is longer than one line, the \u201cpretty printer\u201d adds line breaks and indentation to more clearly reveal data structure:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> pprint<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span> =<\/span> [[[[<\/span>'black'<\/span>,<\/span> 'cyan'<\/span>],<\/span> 'white'<\/span>,<\/span> [<\/span>'green'<\/span>,<\/span> 'red'<\/span>]],<\/span> [[<\/span>'magenta'<\/span>,<\/span>\n... <\/span>    'yellow'<\/span>],<\/span> 'blue'<\/span>]]]<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>pprint<\/span>.<\/span>pprint<\/span>(<\/span>t<\/span>,<\/span> width<\/span>=<\/span>30<\/span>)<\/span>\n[[[['black', 'cyan'],<\/span>\n   'white',<\/span>\n   ['green', 'red']],<\/span>\n  [['magenta', 'yellow'],<\/span>\n   'blue']]]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The\u00a0textwrap<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module formats paragraphs of text to fit a given screen width:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> textwrap<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>doc<\/span> =<\/span> \"\"\"The wrap() method is just like fill() except that it returns<\/span>\n... <\/span>a list of strings instead of one big string with newlines to separate<\/span>\n... <\/span>the wrapped lines.\"\"\"<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>textwrap<\/span>.<\/span>fill<\/span>(<\/span>doc<\/span>,<\/span> width<\/span>=<\/span>40<\/span>))<\/span>\nThe wrap() method is just like fill()<\/span>\nexcept that it returns a list of strings<\/span>\ninstead of one big string with newlines<\/span>\nto separate the wrapped lines.<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The\u00a0locale<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module accesses a database of culture specific data formats. The grouping attribute of locale\u2019s format function provides a direct way of formatting numbers with group separators:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> locale<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>locale<\/span>.<\/span>setlocale<\/span>(<\/span>locale<\/span>.<\/span>LC_ALL<\/span>,<\/span> 'English_United States.1252'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'English_United States.1252'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>conv<\/span> =<\/span> locale<\/span>.<\/span>localeconv<\/span>()<\/span>          # get a mapping of conventions<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>x<\/span> =<\/span> 1234567.8<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>locale<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>\"<\/span>%d<\/span>\"<\/span>,<\/span> x<\/span>,<\/span> grouping<\/span>=<\/span>True<\/span>)<\/span>\n'1,234,567'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>locale<\/span>.<\/span>format_string<\/span>(<\/span>\"<\/span>%s%.*f<\/span>\"<\/span>,<\/span> (<\/span>conv<\/span>[<\/span>'currency_symbol'<\/span>],<\/span>\n... <\/span>                     conv<\/span>[<\/span>'frac_digits'<\/span>],<\/span> x<\/span>),<\/span> grouping<\/span>=<\/span>True<\/span>)<\/span>\n'$1,234,567.80'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.2.\u00a0<\/span>Templating<\/h2>
                              The\u00a0string<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module includes a versatile\u00a0Template<\/span><\/code>\u00a0class with a simplified syntax suitable for editing by end-users. This allows users to customize their applications without having to alter the application.<\/p>
                              The format uses placeholder names formed by\u00a0$<\/span><\/code>\u00a0with valid Python identifiers (alphanumeric characters and underscores). Surrounding the placeholder with braces allows it to be followed by more alphanumeric letters with no intervening spaces. Writing\u00a0$<\/span><\/code>\u00a0creates a single escaped\u00a0$<\/span><\/code>:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>from<\/span> string<\/span> import<\/span> Template<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span> =<\/span> Template<\/span>(<\/span>'$<\/span>{village}<\/span>folk send $10 to $cause.'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span>.<\/span>substitute<\/span>(<\/span>village<\/span>=<\/span>'Nottingham'<\/span>,<\/span> cause<\/span>=<\/span>'the ditch fund'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'Nottinghamfolk send $10 to the ditch fund.'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The\u00a0substitute()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method raises a\u00a0KeyError<\/span><\/code>\u00a0when a placeholder is not supplied in a dictionary or a keyword argument. For mail-merge style applications, user supplied data may be incomplete and the\u00a0safe_substitute()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method may be more appropriate \u2014 it will leave placeholders unchanged if data is missing:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>t<\/span> =<\/span> Template<\/span>(<\/span>'Return the $item to $owner.'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span> =<\/span> dict<\/span>(<\/span>item<\/span>=<\/span>'unladen swallow'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span>.<\/span>substitute<\/span>(<\/span>d<\/span>)<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  ...<\/span>\nKeyError<\/span>: 'owner'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span>.<\/span>safe_substitute<\/span>(<\/span>d<\/span>)<\/span>\n'Return the unladen swallow to $owner.'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              Template subclasses can specify a custom delimiter. For example, a batch renaming utility for a photo browser may elect to use percent signs for placeholders such as the current date, image sequence number, or file format:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> time<\/span>,<\/span> os.path<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>photofiles<\/span> =<\/span> [<\/span>'img_1074.jpg'<\/span>,<\/span> 'img_1076.jpg'<\/span>,<\/span> 'img_1077.jpg'<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>class<\/span> BatchRename<\/span>(<\/span>Template<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>    delimiter<\/span> =<\/span> '%'<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>fmt<\/span> =<\/span> input<\/span>(<\/span>'Enter rename style (<\/span>%d<\/span>-date %n-seqnum <\/span>%f<\/span>-format):  '<\/span>)<\/span>\nEnter rename style (%d-date %n-seqnum %f-format):  Ashley_%n%f<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>t<\/span> =<\/span> BatchRename<\/span>(<\/span>fmt<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>date<\/span> =<\/span> time<\/span>.<\/span>strftime<\/span>(<\/span>'<\/span>%d<\/span>%b%y'<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>for<\/span> i<\/span>,<\/span> filename<\/span> in<\/span> enumerate<\/span>(<\/span>photofiles<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>    base<\/span>,<\/span> ext<\/span> =<\/span> os<\/span>.<\/span>path<\/span>.<\/span>splitext<\/span>(<\/span>filename<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    newname<\/span> =<\/span> t<\/span>.<\/span>substitute<\/span>(<\/span>d<\/span>=<\/span>date<\/span>,<\/span> n<\/span>=<\/span>i<\/span>,<\/span> f<\/span>=<\/span>ext<\/span>)<\/span>\n... <\/span>    print<\/span>(<\/span>'<\/span>{0}<\/span> --> <\/span>{1}<\/span>'<\/span>.<\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>filename<\/span>,<\/span> newname<\/span>))<\/span>\n\nimg_1074.jpg --> Ashley_0.jpg<\/span>\nimg_1076.jpg --> Ashley_1.jpg<\/span>\nimg_1077.jpg --> Ashley_2.jpg<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              Another application for templating is separating program logic from the details of multiple output formats. This makes it possible to substitute custom templates for XML files, plain text reports, and HTML web reports.<\/p><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.3.\u00a0<\/span>Working with Binary Data Record Layouts<\/h2>
                              The\u00a0struct<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides\u00a0pack()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0unpack()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0functions for working with variable length binary record formats. The following example shows how to loop through header information in a ZIP file without using the\u00a0zipfile<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module. Pack codes\u00a0\"H\"<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0\"I\"<\/span><\/code>\u00a0represent two and four byte unsigned numbers respectively. The\u00a0\"<\"<\/span><\/code>\u00a0indicates that they are standard size and in little-endian byte order:<\/p>
                              import<\/span> struct<\/span>\n\nwith<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>'myfile.zip'<\/span>,<\/span> 'rb'<\/span>)<\/span> as<\/span> f<\/span>:<\/span>\n    data<\/span> =<\/span> f<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>()<\/span>\n\nstart<\/span> =<\/span> 0<\/span>\nfor<\/span> i<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>3<\/span>):<\/span>                      # show the first 3 file headers<\/span>\n    start<\/span> +=<\/span> 14<\/span>\n    fields<\/span> =<\/span> struct<\/span>.<\/span>unpack<\/span>(<\/span>'<IIIHH'<\/span>,<\/span> data<\/span>[<\/span>start<\/span>:<\/span>start<\/span>+<\/span>16<\/span>])<\/span>\n    crc32<\/span>,<\/span> comp_size<\/span>,<\/span> uncomp_size<\/span>,<\/span> filenamesize<\/span>,<\/span> extra_size<\/span> =<\/span> fields<\/span>\n\n    start<\/span> +=<\/span> 16<\/span>\n    filename<\/span> =<\/span> data<\/span>[<\/span>start<\/span>:<\/span>start<\/span>+<\/span>filenamesize<\/span>]<\/span>\n    start<\/span> +=<\/span> filenamesize<\/span>\n    extra<\/span> =<\/span> data<\/span>[<\/span>start<\/span>:<\/span>start<\/span>+<\/span>extra_size<\/span>]<\/span>\n    print<\/span>(<\/span>filename<\/span>,<\/span> hex<\/span>(<\/span>crc32<\/span>),<\/span> comp_size<\/span>,<\/span> uncomp_size<\/span>)<\/span>\n\n    start<\/span> +=<\/span> extra_size<\/span> +<\/span> comp_size<\/span>     # skip to the next header<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.4.\u00a0<\/span>Multi-threading<\/h2>
                              Threading is a technique for decoupling tasks which are not sequentially dependent. Threads can be used to improve the responsiveness of applications that accept user input while other tasks run in the background. A related use case is running I\/O in parallel with computations in another thread.<\/p>
                              The following code shows how the high level\u00a0threading<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module can run tasks in background while the main program continues to run:<\/p>
                              import<\/span> threading<\/span>,<\/span> zipfile<\/span>\n\nclass<\/span> AsyncZip<\/span>(<\/span>threading<\/span>.<\/span>Thread<\/span>):<\/span>\n    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> infile<\/span>,<\/span> outfile<\/span>):<\/span>\n        threading<\/span>.<\/span>Thread<\/span>.<\/span>__init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>)<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>infile<\/span> =<\/span> infile<\/span>\n        self<\/span>.<\/span>outfile<\/span> =<\/span> outfile<\/span>\n\n    def<\/span> run<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n        f<\/span> =<\/span> zipfile<\/span>.<\/span>ZipFile<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>.<\/span>outfile<\/span>,<\/span> 'w'<\/span>,<\/span> zipfile<\/span>.<\/span>ZIP_DEFLATED<\/span>)<\/span>\n        f<\/span>.<\/span>write<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>.<\/span>infile<\/span>)<\/span>\n        f<\/span>.<\/span>close<\/span>()<\/span>\n        print<\/span>(<\/span>'Finished background zip of:'<\/span>,<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>infile<\/span>)<\/span>\n\nbackground<\/span> =<\/span> AsyncZip<\/span>(<\/span>'mydata.txt'<\/span>,<\/span> 'myarchive.zip'<\/span>)<\/span>\nbackground<\/span>.<\/span>start<\/span>()<\/span>\nprint<\/span>(<\/span>'The main program continues to run in foreground.'<\/span>)<\/span>\n\nbackground<\/span>.<\/span>join<\/span>()<\/span>    # Wait for the background task to finish<\/span>\nprint<\/span>(<\/span>'Main program waited until background was done.'<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The principal challenge of multi-threaded applications is coordinating threads that share data or other resources. To that end, the threading module provides a number of synchronization primitives including locks, events, condition variables, and semaphores.<\/p>
                              While those tools are powerful, minor design errors can result in problems that are difficult to reproduce. So, the preferred approach to task coordination is to concentrate all access to a resource in a single thread and then use the\u00a0queue<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module to feed that thread with requests from other threads. Applications using\u00a0Queue<\/span><\/code>\u00a0objects for inter-thread communication and coordination are easier to design, more readable, and more reliable.<\/p><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.5.\u00a0<\/span>Logging<\/h2>
                              The\u00a0logging<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module offers a full featured and flexible logging system. At its simplest, log messages are sent to a file or to\u00a0sys.stderr<\/span><\/code>:<\/p>
                              import<\/span> logging<\/span>\nlogging<\/span>.<\/span>debug<\/span>(<\/span>'Debugging information'<\/span>)<\/span>\nlogging<\/span>.<\/span>info<\/span>(<\/span>'Informational message'<\/span>)<\/span>\nlogging<\/span>.<\/span>warning<\/span>(<\/span>'Warning:config file <\/span>%s<\/span> not found'<\/span>,<\/span> 'server.conf'<\/span>)<\/span>\nlogging<\/span>.<\/span>error<\/span>(<\/span>'Error occurred'<\/span>)<\/span>\nlogging<\/span>.<\/span>critical<\/span>(<\/span>'Critical error -- shutting down'<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              This produces the following output:<\/p>
                              WARNING:root:Warning:config file server.conf not found\nERROR:root:Error occurred\nCRITICAL:root:Critical error -- shutting down\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              By default, informational and debugging messages are suppressed and the output is sent to standard error. Other output options include routing messages through email, datagrams, sockets, or to an HTTP Server. New filters can select different routing based on message priority:\u00a0DEBUG<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0INFO<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0WARNING<\/span><\/code>,\u00a0ERROR<\/span><\/code>, and\u00a0CRITICAL<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                              The logging system can be configured directly from Python or can be loaded from a user editable configuration file for customized logging without altering the application.<\/p><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.6.\u00a0<\/span>Weak References<\/h2>
                              Python does automatic memory management (reference counting for most objects and\u00a0garbage collection<\/span>\u00a0to eliminate cycles). The memory is freed shortly after the last reference to it has been eliminated.<\/p>
                              This approach works fine for most applications but occasionally there is a need to track objects only as long as they are being used by something else. Unfortunately, just tracking them creates a reference that makes them permanent. The\u00a0weakref<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides tools for tracking objects without creating a reference. When the object is no longer needed, it is automatically removed from a weakref table and a callback is triggered for weakref objects. Typical applications include caching objects that are expensive to create:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> weakref<\/span>,<\/span> gc<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>class<\/span> A<\/span>:<\/span>\n... <\/span>    def<\/span> __init__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>,<\/span> value<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>        self<\/span>.<\/span>value<\/span> =<\/span> value<\/span>\n... <\/span>    def<\/span> __repr__<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>):<\/span>\n... <\/span>        return<\/span> str<\/span>(<\/span>self<\/span>.<\/span>value<\/span>)<\/span>\n...<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>a<\/span> =<\/span> A<\/span>(<\/span>10<\/span>)<\/span>                   # create a reference<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span> =<\/span> weakref<\/span>.<\/span>WeakValueDictionary<\/span>()<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span>[<\/span>'primary'<\/span>]<\/span> =<\/span> a<\/span>            # does not create a reference<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span>[<\/span>'primary'<\/span>]<\/span>                # fetch the object if it is still alive<\/span>\n10<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>del<\/span> a<\/span>                       # remove the one reference<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>gc<\/span>.<\/span>collect<\/span>()<\/span>                # run garbage collection right away<\/span>\n0<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span>[<\/span>'primary'<\/span>]<\/span>                # entry was automatically removed<\/span>\nTraceback (most recent call last):<\/span>\n  File \"\"<\/span>, line 1<\/span>, in \n    d<\/span>[<\/span>'primary'<\/span>]<\/span>                # entry was automatically removed<\/span>\n  File \"C:\/python39\/lib\/weakref.py\"<\/span>, line 46<\/span>, in __getitem__<\/span>\n    o<\/span> =<\/span> self<\/span>.<\/span>data<\/span>[<\/span>key<\/span>]()<\/span>\nKeyError<\/span>: 'primary'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
