                              11.7.\u00a0<\/span>Tools for Working with Lists<\/h2>
                              Many data structure needs can be met with the built-in list type. However, sometimes there is a need for alternative implementations with different performance trade-offs.<\/p>
                              The\u00a0array<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides an\u00a0array()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0object that is like a list that stores only homogeneous data and stores it more compactly. The following example shows an array of numbers stored as two byte unsigned binary numbers (typecode\u00a0\"H\"<\/span><\/code>) rather than the usual 16 bytes per entry for regular lists of Python int objects:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>from<\/span> array<\/span> import<\/span> array<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>a<\/span> =<\/span> array<\/span>(<\/span>'H'<\/span>,<\/span> [<\/span>4000<\/span>,<\/span> 10<\/span>,<\/span> 700<\/span>,<\/span> 22222<\/span>])<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>sum<\/span>(<\/span>a<\/span>)<\/span>\n26932<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>a<\/span>[<\/span>1<\/span>:<\/span>3<\/span>]<\/span>\narray('H', [10, 700])<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The\u00a0collections<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides a\u00a0deque()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0object that is like a list with faster appends and pops from the left side but slower lookups in the middle. These objects are well suited for implementing queues and breadth first tree searches:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>from<\/span> collections<\/span> import<\/span> deque<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span> =<\/span> deque<\/span>([<\/span>\"task1\"<\/span>,<\/span> \"task2\"<\/span>,<\/span> \"task3\"<\/span>])<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>d<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>\"task4\"<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>print<\/span>(<\/span>\"Handling\"<\/span>,<\/span> d<\/span>.<\/span>popleft<\/span>())<\/span>\nHandling task1<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              unsearched<\/span> =<\/span> deque<\/span>([<\/span>starting_node<\/span>])<\/span>\ndef<\/span> breadth_first_search<\/span>(<\/span>unsearched<\/span>):<\/span>\n    node<\/span> =<\/span> unsearched<\/span>.<\/span>popleft<\/span>()<\/span>\n    for<\/span> m<\/span> in<\/span> gen_moves<\/span>(<\/span>node<\/span>):<\/span>\n        if<\/span> is_goal<\/span>(<\/span>m<\/span>):<\/span>\n            return<\/span> m<\/span>\n        unsearched<\/span>.<\/span>append<\/span>(<\/span>m<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              In addition to alternative list implementations, the library also offers other tools such as the\u00a0bisect<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module with functions for manipulating sorted lists:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>import<\/span> bisect<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>scores<\/span> =<\/span> [(<\/span>100<\/span>,<\/span> 'perl'<\/span>),<\/span> (<\/span>200<\/span>,<\/span> 'tcl'<\/span>),<\/span> (<\/span>400<\/span>,<\/span> 'lua'<\/span>),<\/span> (<\/span>500<\/span>,<\/span> 'python'<\/span>)]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>bisect<\/span>.<\/span>insort<\/span>(<\/span>scores<\/span>,<\/span> (<\/span>300<\/span>,<\/span> 'ruby'<\/span>))<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>scores<\/span>\n[(100, 'perl'), (200, 'tcl'), (300, 'ruby'), (400, 'lua'), (500, 'python')]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                              The\u00a0heapq<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides functions for implementing heaps based on regular lists. The lowest valued entry is always kept at position zero. This is useful for applications which repeatedly access the smallest element but do not want to run a full list sort:<\/p>
                              >>><\/span><\/p>
                              >>> <\/span>from<\/span> heapq<\/span> import<\/span> heapify<\/span>,<\/span> heappop<\/span>,<\/span> heappush<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>data<\/span> =<\/span> [<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 3<\/span>,<\/span> 5<\/span>,<\/span> 7<\/span>,<\/span> 9<\/span>,<\/span> 2<\/span>,<\/span> 4<\/span>,<\/span> 6<\/span>,<\/span> 8<\/span>,<\/span> 0<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>heapify<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span>                      # rearrange the list into heap order<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>heappush<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>,<\/span> -<\/span>5<\/span>)<\/span>                 # add a new entry<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>[<\/span>heappop<\/span>(<\/span>data<\/span>)<\/span> for<\/span> i<\/span> in<\/span> range<\/span>(<\/span>3<\/span>)]<\/span>  # fetch the three smallest entries<\/span>\n[-5, 0, 1]<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                              \u00a0<\/p>
                              11.8.\u00a0<\/span>Decimal Floating Point Arithmetic<\/h2>
                              The\u00a0decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module offers a\u00a0Decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0datatype for decimal floating point arithmetic. Compared to the built-in\u00a0float<\/span><\/code>\u00a0implementation of binary floating point, the class is especially helpful for<\/p>
                              • financial applications and other uses which require exact decimal representation,<\/p><\/li>
                              • control over precision,<\/p><\/li>
                              • control over rounding to meet legal or regulatory requirements,<\/p><\/li>
                              • tracking of significant decimal places, or<\/p><\/li>
                              • applications where the user expects the results to match calculations done by hand.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                                For example, calculating a 5% tax on a 70 cent phone charge gives different results in decimal floating point and binary floating point. The difference becomes significant if the results are rounded to the nearest cent:<\/p>
                                >>><\/span><\/p>
                                >>> <\/span>from<\/span> decimal<\/span> import<\/span> *<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>round<\/span>(<\/span>Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>'0.70'<\/span>)<\/span> *<\/span> Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>'1.05'<\/span>),<\/span> 2<\/span>)<\/span>\nDecimal('0.74')<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>70<\/span> *<\/span> 1.05<\/span>,<\/span> 2<\/span>)<\/span>\n0.73<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                The\u00a0Decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0result keeps a trailing zero, automatically inferring four place significance from multiplicands with two place significance. Decimal reproduces mathematics as done by hand and avoids issues that can arise when binary floating point cannot exactly represent decimal quantities.<\/p>
                                Exact representation enables the\u00a0Decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0class to perform modulo calculations and equality tests that are unsuitable for binary floating point:<\/p>
                                >>><\/span><\/p>
                                >>> <\/span>Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>'1.00'<\/span>)<\/span> %<\/span> Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>'.10'<\/span>)<\/span>\nDecimal('0.00')<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>1.00<\/span> %<\/span> 0.10<\/span>\n0.09999999999999995<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>sum<\/span>([<\/span>Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>'0.1'<\/span>)]<\/span>*<\/span>10<\/span>)<\/span> ==<\/span> Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>'1.0'<\/span>)<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>sum<\/span>([<\/span>0.1<\/span>]<\/span>*<\/span>10<\/span>)<\/span> ==<\/span> 1.0<\/span>\nFalse<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                The\u00a0decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module provides arithmetic with as much precision as needed:<\/p>
                                >>><\/span><\/p>
                                >>> <\/span>getcontext<\/span>()<\/span>.<\/span>prec<\/span> =<\/span> 36<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>1<\/span>)<\/span> \/<\/span> Decimal<\/span>(<\/span>7<\/span>)<\/span>\nDecimal('0.142857142857142857142857142857142857')<\/span><\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                12.\u00a0<\/span>Virtual Environments and Packages<\/h2>
                                12.1.\u00a0<\/span>Introduction<\/h2>
                                Python applications will often use packages and modules that don\u2019t come as part of the standard library. Applications will sometimes need a specific version of a library, because the application may require that a particular bug has been fixed or the application may be written using an obsolete version of the library\u2019s interface.<\/p>
                                This means it may not be possible for one Python installation to meet the requirements of every application. If application A needs version 1.0 of a particular module but application B needs version 2.0, then the requirements are in conflict and installing either version 1.0 or 2.0 will leave one application unable to run.<\/p>
                                The solution for this problem is to create a\u00a0virtual environment<\/span>, a self-contained directory tree that contains a Python installation for a particular version of Python, plus a number of additional packages.<\/p>
                                Different applications can then use different virtual environments. To resolve the earlier example of conflicting requirements, application A can have its own virtual environment with version 1.0 installed while application B has another virtual environment with version 2.0. If application B requires a library be upgraded to version 3.0, this will not affect application A\u2019s environment.<\/p><\/div>
                                12.2.\u00a0<\/span>Creating Virtual Environments<\/h2>
                                The module used to create and manage virtual environments is called\u00a0venv<\/span><\/code>.\u00a0venv<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will usually install the most recent version of Python that you have available. If you have multiple versions of Python on your system, you can select a specific Python version by running\u00a0python3<\/span><\/code>\u00a0or whichever version you want.<\/p>
                                To create a virtual environment, decide upon a directory where you want to place it, and run the\u00a0venv<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module as a script with the directory path:<\/p>
                                python3<\/span> -<\/span>m<\/span> venv<\/span> tutorial<\/span>-<\/span>env<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                This will create the\u00a0tutorial-env<\/span><\/code>\u00a0directory if it doesn\u2019t exist, and also create directories inside it containing a copy of the Python interpreter, the standard library, and various supporting files.<\/p>
                                A common directory location for a virtual environment is\u00a0.venv<\/span><\/code>. This name keeps the directory typically hidden in your shell and thus out of the way while giving it a name that explains why the directory exists. It also prevents clashing with\u00a0.env<\/span><\/code>\u00a0environment variable definition files that some tooling supports.<\/p>
                                Once you\u2019ve created a virtual environment, you may activate it.<\/p>
                                On Windows, run:<\/p>
                                tutorial<\/span>-<\/span>env<\/span>\\Scripts<\/span>\\activate<\/span>.<\/span>bat<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                On Unix or MacOS, run:<\/p>
                                source<\/span> tutorial<\/span>-<\/span>env<\/span>\/<\/span>bin<\/span>\/<\/span>activate<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                (This script is written for the bash shell. If you use the\u00a0csh<\/strong>\u00a0or\u00a0fish<\/strong>\u00a0shells, there are alternate\u00a0activate.csh<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0activate.fish<\/span><\/code>\u00a0scripts you should use instead.)<\/p>
                                Activating the virtual environment will change your shell\u2019s prompt to show what virtual environment you\u2019re using, and modify the environment so that running\u00a0python<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will get you that particular version and installation of Python. For example:<\/p>
                                $ source<\/span> ~\/envs\/tutorial-env\/bin\/activate\n(<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ python\nPython 3<\/span>.5.1 (<\/span>default, May  6<\/span> 2016<\/span>, 10<\/span>:59:36)<\/span>\n  ...\n>>> import sys\n>>> sys.path\n[<\/span>''<\/span>, '\/usr\/local\/lib\/python35.zip'<\/span>, ...,\n'~\/envs\/tutorial-env\/lib\/python3.5\/site-packages'<\/span>]<\/span>\n>>>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                                12.3.\u00a0<\/span>Managing Packages with pip<\/h2>
                                You can install, upgrade, and remove packages using a program called\u00a0pip<\/strong>. By default\u00a0pip<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will install packages from the Python Package Index, <https:\/\/pypi.org>. You can browse the Python Package Index by going to it in your web browser, or you can use\u00a0pip<\/span><\/code>\u2019s limited search feature:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ pip search astronomy\nskyfield               - Elegant astronomy for<\/span> Python\ngary                   - Galactic astronomy and gravitational dynamics.\nnovas                  - The United States Naval Observatory NOVAS astronomy library\nastroobs               - Provides astronomy ephemeris to plan telescope observations\nPyAstronomy            - A collection of astronomy related tools for<\/span> Python.\n...\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                pip<\/span><\/code>\u00a0has a number of subcommands: \u201csearch\u201d, \u201cinstall\u201d, \u201cuninstall\u201d, \u201cfreeze\u201d, etc. (Consult the\u00a0Installing Python Modules<\/span>\u00a0guide for complete documentation for\u00a0pip<\/span><\/code>.)<\/p>
                                You can install the latest version of a package by specifying a package\u2019s name:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ python -m pip install novas\nCollecting novas\n  Downloading novas-3.1.1.3.tar.gz (<\/span>136kB)<\/span>\nInstalling collected packages: novas\n  Running setup.py install for<\/span> novas\nSuccessfully installed novas-3.1.1.3\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                You can also install a specific version of a package by giving the package name followed by\u00a0==<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and the version number:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ python -m pip install requests<\/span>==<\/span>2<\/span>.6.0\nCollecting requests<\/span>==<\/span>2<\/span>.6.0\n  Using cached requests-2.6.0-py2.py3-none-any.whl\nInstalling collected packages: requests\nSuccessfully installed requests-2.6.0\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                If you re-run this command,\u00a0pip<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will notice that the requested version is already installed and do nothing. You can supply a different version number to get that version, or you can run\u00a0pip<\/span>\u00a0install<\/span>\u00a0--upgrade<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to upgrade the package to the latest version:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ python -m pip install --upgrade requests\nCollecting requests\nInstalling collected packages: requests\n  Found existing installation: requests 2<\/span>.6.0\n    Uninstalling requests-2.6.0:\n      Successfully uninstalled requests-2.6.0\nSuccessfully installed requests-2.7.0\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                pip<\/span>\u00a0uninstall<\/span><\/code>\u00a0followed by one or more package names will remove the packages from the virtual environment.<\/p>
                                pip<\/span>\u00a0show<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will display information about a particular package:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ pip show requests\n---\nMetadata-Version: 2<\/span>.0\nName: requests\nVersion: 2<\/span>.7.0\nSummary: Python HTTP for<\/span> Humans.\nHome-page: http:\/\/python-requests.org\nAuthor: Kenneth Reitz\nAuthor-email: me@kennethreitz.com\nLicense: Apache 2<\/span>.0\nLocation: \/Users\/akuchling\/envs\/tutorial-env\/lib\/python3.4\/site-packages\nRequires:\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                pip<\/span>\u00a0list<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will display all of the packages installed in the virtual environment:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ pip list\nnovas (<\/span>3<\/span>.1.1.3)<\/span>\nnumpy (<\/span>1<\/span>.9.2)<\/span>\npip (<\/span>7<\/span>.0.3)<\/span>\nrequests (<\/span>2<\/span>.7.0)<\/span>\nsetuptools (<\/span>16<\/span>.0)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                pip<\/span>\u00a0freeze<\/span><\/code>\u00a0will produce a similar list of the installed packages, but the output uses the format that\u00a0pip<\/span>\u00a0install<\/span><\/code>\u00a0expects. A common convention is to put this list in a\u00a0requirements.txt<\/span><\/code>\u00a0file:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ pip freeze > requirements.txt\n(<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ cat requirements.txt\nnovas<\/span>==<\/span>3<\/span>.1.1.3\nnumpy<\/span>==<\/span>1<\/span>.9.2\nrequests<\/span>==<\/span>2<\/span>.7.0\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                The\u00a0requirements.txt<\/span><\/code>\u00a0can then be committed to version control and shipped as part of an application. Users can then install all the necessary packages with\u00a0install<\/span>\u00a0-r<\/span><\/code>:<\/p>
                                (<\/span>tutorial-env)<\/span> $ python -m pip install -r requirements.txt\nCollecting novas<\/span>==<\/span>3<\/span>.1.1.3 (<\/span>from -r requirements.txt (<\/span>line 1<\/span>))<\/span>\n  ...\nCollecting numpy<\/span>==<\/span>1<\/span>.9.2 (<\/span>from -r requirements.txt (<\/span>line 2<\/span>))<\/span>\n  ...\nCollecting requests<\/span>==<\/span>2<\/span>.7.0 (<\/span>from -r requirements.txt (<\/span>line 3<\/span>))<\/span>\n  ...\nInstalling collected packages: novas, numpy, requests\n  Running setup.py install for<\/span> novas\nSuccessfully installed novas-3.1.1.3 numpy-1.9.2 requests-2.7.0\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                pip<\/span><\/code>\u00a0has many more options. Consult the\u00a0Installing Python Modules<\/span>\u00a0guide for complete documentation for\u00a0pip<\/span><\/code>. When you\u2019ve written a package and want to make it available on the Python Package Index, consult the\u00a0Distributing Python Modules<\/span>\u00a0guide.<\/p><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t

                                13.\u00a0<\/span>What Now?<\/h2>
                                Reading this tutorial has probably reinforced your interest in using Python \u2014 you should be eager to apply Python to solving your real-world problems. Where should you go to learn more?<\/p>
                                This tutorial is part of Python\u2019s documentation set. Some other documents in the set are:<\/p>
                                • The Python Standard Library<\/span>:<\/p>
                                  You should browse through this manual, which gives complete (though terse) reference material about types, functions, and the modules in the standard library. The standard Python distribution includes a\u00a0lot<\/em>\u00a0of additional code. There are modules to read Unix mailboxes, retrieve documents via HTTP, generate random numbers, parse command-line options, write CGI programs, compress data, and many other tasks. Skimming through the Library Reference will give you an idea of what\u2019s available.<\/p><\/li>
                                • Installing Python Modules<\/span>\u00a0explains how to install additional modules written by other Python users.<\/p><\/li>
                                • The Python Language Reference<\/span>: A detailed explanation of Python\u2019s syntax and semantics. It\u2019s heavy reading, but is useful as a complete guide to the language itself.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>
                                  More Python resources:<\/p>
                                  • https:\/\/www.python.org: The major Python Web site. It contains code, documentation, and pointers to Python-related pages around the Web. This Web site is mirrored in various places around the world, such as Europe, Japan, and Australia; a mirror may be faster than the main site, depending on your geographical location.<\/p><\/li>
                                  • https:\/\/docs.python.org: Fast access to Python\u2019s documentation.<\/p><\/li>
                                  • https:\/\/pypi.org: The Python Package Index, previously also nicknamed the Cheese Shop\u00a01, is an index of user-created Python modules that are available for download. Once you begin releasing code, you can register it here so that others can find it.<\/p><\/li>
                                  • https:\/\/code.activestate.com\/recipes\/langs\/python\/: The Python Cookbook is a sizable collection of code examples, larger modules, and useful scripts. Particularly notable contributions are collected in a book also titled Python Cookbook (O\u2019Reilly & Associates, ISBN 0-596-00797-3.)<\/p><\/li>
                                  • http:\/\/www.pyvideo.org\u00a0collects links to Python-related videos from conferences and user-group meetings.<\/p><\/li>
                                  • https:\/\/scipy.org: The Scientific Python project includes modules for fast array computations and manipulations plus a host of packages for such things as linear algebra, Fourier transforms, non-linear solvers, random number distributions, statistical analysis and the like.<\/p><\/li><\/ul>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t

                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                    14.\u00a0<\/span>Interactive Input Editing and History Substitution<\/h2>
                                    Some versions of the Python interpreter support editing of the current input line and history substitution, similar to facilities found in the Korn shell and the GNU Bash shell. This is implemented using the\u00a0GNU Readline\u00a0library, which supports various styles of editing. This library has its own documentation which we won\u2019t duplicate here.<\/p>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    14.1.\u00a0<\/span>Tab Completion and History Editing<\/h2>
                                    Completion of variable and module names is\u00a0automatically enabled<\/span>\u00a0at interpreter startup so that the\u00a0Tab<\/kbd>\u00a0key invokes the completion function; it looks at Python statement names, the current local variables, and the available module names. For dotted expressions such as\u00a0string.a<\/span><\/code>, it will evaluate the expression up to the final\u00a0'.'<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and then suggest completions from the attributes of the resulting object. Note that this may execute application-defined code if an object with a\u00a0__getattr__()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method is part of the expression. The default configuration also saves your history into a file named\u00a0.python_history<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in your user directory. The history will be available again during the next interactive interpreter session.<\/p><\/div>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    14.2.\u00a0<\/span>Alternatives to the Interactive Interpreter<\/h2>
                                    This facility is an enormous step forward compared to earlier versions of the interpreter; however, some wishes are left: It would be nice if the proper indentation were suggested on continuation lines (the parser knows if an indent token is required next). The completion mechanism might use the interpreter\u2019s symbol table. A command to check (or even suggest) matching parentheses, quotes, etc., would also be useful.<\/p>
                                    One alternative enhanced interactive interpreter that has been around for quite some time is\u00a0IPython, which features tab completion, object exploration and advanced history management. It can also be thoroughly customized and embedded into other applications. Another similar enhanced interactive environment is\u00a0bpython.<\/p><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                    15.\u00a0<\/span>Floating Point Arithmetic: Issues and Limitations<\/h2>
                                    Floating-point numbers are represented in computer hardware as base 2 (binary) fractions. For example, the decimal fraction<\/p>
                                    0.125<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    has value 1\/10 + 2\/100 + 5\/1000, and in the same way the binary fraction<\/p>
                                    0.001<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    has value 0\/2 + 0\/4 + 1\/8. These two fractions have identical values, the only real difference being that the first is written in base 10 fractional notation, and the second in base 2.<\/p>
                                    Unfortunately, most decimal fractions cannot be represented exactly as binary fractions. A consequence is that, in general, the decimal floating-point numbers you enter are only approximated by the binary floating-point numbers actually stored in the machine.<\/p>
                                    The problem is easier to understand at first in base 10. Consider the fraction 1\/3. You can approximate that as a base 10 fraction:<\/p>
                                    0.3<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    or, better,<\/p>
                                    0.33<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    or, better,<\/p>
                                    0.333<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    and so on. No matter how many digits you\u2019re willing to write down, the result will never be exactly 1\/3, but will be an increasingly better approximation of 1\/3.<\/p>
                                    In the same way, no matter how many base 2 digits you\u2019re willing to use, the decimal value 0.1 cannot be represented exactly as a base 2 fraction. In base 2, 1\/10 is the infinitely repeating fraction<\/p>
                                    0.0001100110011001100110011001100110011001100110011<\/span>...<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Stop at any finite number of bits, and you get an approximation. On most machines today, floats are approximated using a binary fraction with the numerator using the first 53 bits starting with the most significant bit and with the denominator as a power of two. In the case of 1\/10, the binary fraction is\u00a03602879701896397<\/span>\u00a0\/<\/span>\u00a02<\/span>\u00a0**<\/span>\u00a055<\/span><\/code>\u00a0which is close to but not exactly equal to the true value of 1\/10.<\/p>
                                    Many users are not aware of the approximation because of the way values are displayed. Python only prints a decimal approximation to the true decimal value of the binary approximation stored by the machine. On most machines, if Python were to print the true decimal value of the binary approximation stored for 0.1, it would have to display<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>0.1<\/span>\n0.1000000000000000055511151231257827021181583404541015625<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    That is more digits than most people find useful, so Python keeps the number of digits manageable by displaying a rounded value instead<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>1<\/span> \/<\/span> 10<\/span>\n0.1<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Just remember, even though the printed result looks like the exact value of 1\/10, the actual stored value is the nearest representable binary fraction.<\/p>
                                    Interestingly, there are many different decimal numbers that share the same nearest approximate binary fraction. For example, the numbers\u00a00.1<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a00.10000000000000001<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a00.1000000000000000055511151231257827021181583404541015625<\/span><\/code>\u00a0are all approximated by\u00a03602879701896397<\/span>\u00a0\/<\/span>\u00a02<\/span>\u00a0**<\/span>\u00a055<\/span><\/code>. Since all of these decimal values share the same approximation, any one of them could be displayed while still preserving the invariant\u00a0eval(repr(x))<\/span>\u00a0==<\/span>\u00a0x<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                                    Historically, the Python prompt and built-in\u00a0repr()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function would choose the one with 17 significant digits,\u00a00.10000000000000001<\/span><\/code>. Starting with Python 3.1, Python (on most systems) is now able to choose the shortest of these and simply display\u00a00.1<\/span><\/code>.<\/p>
                                    Note that this is in the very nature of binary floating-point: this is not a bug in Python, and it is not a bug in your code either. You\u2019ll see the same kind of thing in all languages that support your hardware\u2019s floating-point arithmetic (although some languages may not\u00a0display<\/em>\u00a0the difference by default, or in all output modes).<\/p>
                                    For more pleasant output, you may wish to use string formatting to produce a limited number of significant digits:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>pi<\/span>,<\/span> '.12g'<\/span>)<\/span>  # give 12 significant digits<\/span>\n'3.14159265359'<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>pi<\/span>,<\/span> '.2f'<\/span>)<\/span>   # give 2 digits after the point<\/span>\n'3.14'<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>repr<\/span>(<\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>pi<\/span>)<\/span>\n'3.141592653589793'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    It\u2019s important to realize that this is, in a real sense, an illusion: you\u2019re simply rounding the\u00a0display<\/em>\u00a0of the true machine value.<\/p>
                                    One illusion may beget another. For example, since 0.1 is not exactly 1\/10, summing three values of 0.1 may not yield exactly 0.3, either:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>.<\/span>1<\/span> +<\/span> .<\/span>1<\/span> +<\/span> .<\/span>1<\/span> ==<\/span> .<\/span>3<\/span>\nFalse<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Also, since the 0.1 cannot get any closer to the exact value of 1\/10 and 0.3 cannot get any closer to the exact value of 3\/10, then pre-rounding with\u00a0round()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function cannot help:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 1<\/span>)<\/span> +<\/span> round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 1<\/span>)<\/span> +<\/span> round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 1<\/span>)<\/span> ==<\/span> round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>3<\/span>,<\/span> 1<\/span>)<\/span>\nFalse<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Though the numbers cannot be made closer to their intended exact values, the\u00a0round()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function can be useful for post-rounding so that results with inexact values become comparable to one another:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>1<\/span> +<\/span> .<\/span>1<\/span> +<\/span> .<\/span>1<\/span>,<\/span> 10<\/span>)<\/span> ==<\/span> round<\/span>(<\/span>.<\/span>3<\/span>,<\/span> 10<\/span>)<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Binary floating-point arithmetic holds many surprises like this. The problem with \u201c0.1\u201d is explained in precise detail below, in the \u201cRepresentation Error\u201d section. See\u00a0The Perils of Floating Point\u00a0for a more complete account of other common surprises.<\/p>
                                    As that says near the end, \u201cthere are no easy answers.\u201d Still, don\u2019t be unduly wary of floating-point! The errors in Python float operations are inherited from the floating-point hardware, and on most machines are on the order of no more than 1 part in 2**53 per operation. That\u2019s more than adequate for most tasks, but you do need to keep in mind that it\u2019s not decimal arithmetic and that every float operation can suffer a new rounding error.<\/p>
                                    While pathological cases do exist, for most casual use of floating-point arithmetic you\u2019ll see the result you expect in the end if you simply round the display of your final results to the number of decimal digits you expect.\u00a0str()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0usually suffices, and for finer control see the\u00a0str.format()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method\u2019s format specifiers in\u00a0Format String Syntax<\/span>.<\/p>
                                    For use cases which require exact decimal representation, try using the\u00a0decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module which implements decimal arithmetic suitable for accounting applications and high-precision applications.<\/p>
                                    Another form of exact arithmetic is supported by the\u00a0fractions<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module which implements arithmetic based on rational numbers (so the numbers like 1\/3 can be represented exactly).<\/p>
                                    If you are a heavy user of floating point operations you should take a look at the Numerical Python package and many other packages for mathematical and statistical operations supplied by the SciPy project. See <https:\/\/scipy.org>.<\/p>
                                    Python provides tools that may help on those rare occasions when you really\u00a0do<\/em>\u00a0want to know the exact value of a float. The\u00a0float.as_integer_ratio()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method expresses the value of a float as a fraction:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>x<\/span> =<\/span> 3.14159<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>x<\/span>.<\/span>as_integer_ratio<\/span>()<\/span>\n(3537115888337719, 1125899906842624)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Since the ratio is exact, it can be used to losslessly recreate the original value:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>x<\/span> ==<\/span> 3537115888337719<\/span> \/<\/span> 1125899906842624<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    The\u00a0float.hex()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0method expresses a float in hexadecimal (base 16), again giving the exact value stored by your computer:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>x<\/span>.<\/span>hex<\/span>()<\/span>\n'0x1.921f9f01b866ep+1'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    This precise hexadecimal representation can be used to reconstruct the float value exactly:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>x<\/span> ==<\/span> float<\/span>.<\/span>fromhex<\/span>(<\/span>'0x1.921f9f01b866ep+1'<\/span>)<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Since the representation is exact, it is useful for reliably porting values across different versions of Python (platform independence) and exchanging data with other languages that support the same format (such as Java and C99).<\/p>
                                    Another helpful tool is the\u00a0math.fsum()<\/span><\/code>\u00a0function which helps mitigate loss-of-precision during summation. It tracks \u201clost digits\u201d as values are added onto a running total. That can make a difference in overall accuracy so that the errors do not accumulate to the point where they affect the final total:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>sum<\/span>([<\/span>0.1<\/span>]<\/span> *<\/span> 10<\/span>)<\/span> ==<\/span> 1.0<\/span>\nFalse<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>math<\/span>.<\/span>fsum<\/span>([<\/span>0.1<\/span>]<\/span> *<\/span> 10<\/span>)<\/span> ==<\/span> 1.0<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    15.1.\u00a0<\/span>Representation Error<\/h2>
                                    This section explains the \u201c0.1\u201d example in detail, and shows how you can perform an exact analysis of cases like this yourself. Basic familiarity with binary floating-point representation is assumed.<\/p>
                                    Representation error<\/em>\u00a0refers to the fact that some (most, actually) decimal fractions cannot be represented exactly as binary (base 2) fractions. This is the chief reason why Python (or Perl, C, C++, Java, Fortran, and many others) often won\u2019t display the exact decimal number you expect.<\/p>
                                    Why is that? 1\/10 is not exactly representable as a binary fraction. Almost all machines today (November 2000) use IEEE-754 floating point arithmetic, and almost all platforms map Python floats to IEEE-754 \u201cdouble precision\u201d. 754 doubles contain 53 bits of precision, so on input the computer strives to convert 0.1 to the closest fraction it can of the form\u00a0J<\/em>\/2**N<\/em>\u00a0where\u00a0J<\/em>\u00a0is an integer containing exactly 53 bits. Rewriting<\/p>
                                    1<\/span> \/<\/span> 10<\/span> ~=<\/span> J<\/span> \/<\/span> (<\/span>2<\/span>**<\/span>N<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    as<\/p>
                                    J<\/span> ~=<\/span> 2<\/span>**<\/span>N<\/span> \/<\/span> 10<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    and recalling that\u00a0J<\/em>\u00a0has exactly 53 bits (is\u00a0>=<\/span>\u00a02**52<\/span><\/code>\u00a0but\u00a0<<\/span>\u00a02**53<\/span><\/code>), the best value for\u00a0N<\/em>\u00a0is 56:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>2<\/span>**<\/span>52<\/span> <=<\/span>  2<\/span>**<\/span>56<\/span> \/\/<\/span> 10<\/span>  <<\/span> 2<\/span>**<\/span>53<\/span>\nTrue<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    That is, 56 is the only value for\u00a0N<\/em>\u00a0that leaves\u00a0J<\/em>\u00a0with exactly 53 bits. The best possible value for\u00a0J<\/em>\u00a0is then that quotient rounded:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>q<\/span>,<\/span> r<\/span> =<\/span> divmod<\/span>(<\/span>2<\/span>**<\/span>56<\/span>,<\/span> 10<\/span>)<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>r<\/span>\n6<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Since the remainder is more than half of 10, the best approximation is obtained by rounding up:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>q<\/span>+<\/span>1<\/span>\n7205759403792794<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Therefore the best possible approximation to 1\/10 in 754 double precision is:<\/p>
                                    7205759403792794<\/span> \/<\/span> 2<\/span> **<\/span> 56<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Dividing both the numerator and denominator by two reduces the fraction to:<\/p>
                                    3602879701896397<\/span> \/<\/span> 2<\/span> **<\/span> 55<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Note that since we rounded up, this is actually a little bit larger than 1\/10; if we had not rounded up, the quotient would have been a little bit smaller than 1\/10. But in no case can it be\u00a0exactly<\/em>\u00a01\/10!<\/p>
                                    So the computer never \u201csees\u201d 1\/10: what it sees is the exact fraction given above, the best 754 double approximation it can get:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>0.1<\/span> *<\/span> 2<\/span> **<\/span> 55<\/span>\n3602879701896397.0<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    If we multiply that fraction by 10**55, we can see the value out to 55 decimal digits:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>3602879701896397<\/span> *<\/span> 10<\/span> **<\/span> 55<\/span> \/\/<\/span> 2<\/span> **<\/span> 55<\/span>\n1000000000000000055511151231257827021181583404541015625<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    meaning that the exact number stored in the computer is equal to the decimal value 0.1000000000000000055511151231257827021181583404541015625. Instead of displaying the full decimal value, many languages (including older versions of Python), round the result to 17 significant digits:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>0.1<\/span>,<\/span> '.17f'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'0.10000000000000001'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    The\u00a0fractions<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0decimal<\/span><\/code>\u00a0modules make these calculations easy:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>from<\/span> decimal<\/span> import<\/span> Decimal<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>from<\/span> fractions<\/span> import<\/span> Fraction<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>Fraction<\/span>.<\/span>from_float<\/span>(<\/span>0.1<\/span>)<\/span>\nFraction(3602879701896397, 36028797018963968)<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>(<\/span>0.1<\/span>)<\/span>.<\/span>as_integer_ratio<\/span>()<\/span>\n(3602879701896397, 36028797018963968)<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>Decimal<\/span>.<\/span>from_float<\/span>(<\/span>0.1<\/span>)<\/span>\nDecimal('0.1000000000000000055511151231257827021181583404541015625')<\/span>\n\n>>> <\/span>format<\/span>(<\/span>Decimal<\/span>.<\/span>from_float<\/span>(<\/span>0.1<\/span>),<\/span> '.17'<\/span>)<\/span>\n'0.10000000000000001'<\/span><\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                    16.\u00a0<\/span>Appendix<\/h2>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    16.1.\u00a0<\/span>Interactive Mode<\/h2>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    16.1.1.\u00a0<\/span>Error Handling<\/h3>
                                    When an error occurs, the interpreter prints an error message and a stack trace. In interactive mode, it then returns to the primary prompt; when input came from a file, it exits with a nonzero exit status after printing the stack trace. (Exceptions handled by an\u00a0except<\/span><\/code>\u00a0clause in a\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement are not errors in this context.) Some errors are unconditionally fatal and cause an exit with a nonzero exit; this applies to internal inconsistencies and some cases of running out of memory. All error messages are written to the standard error stream; normal output from executed commands is written to standard output.<\/p>
                                    Typing the interrupt character (usually\u00a0Control-C<\/kbd>\u00a0or\u00a0Delete<\/kbd>) to the primary or secondary prompt cancels the input and returns to the primary prompt.\u00a01\u00a0Typing an interrupt while a command is executing raises the\u00a0KeyboardInterrupt<\/span><\/code>\u00a0exception, which may be handled by a\u00a0try<\/span><\/code>\u00a0statement.<\/p><\/div>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    16.1.2.\u00a0<\/span>Executable Python Scripts<\/h3>
                                    On BSD\u2019ish Unix systems, Python scripts can be made directly executable, like shell scripts, by putting the line<\/p>
                                    #!\/usr\/bin\/env python3.5<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    (assuming that the interpreter is on the user\u2019s\u00a0<\/span>PATH<\/span><\/code>) at the beginning of the script and giving the file an executable mode. The\u00a0#!<\/span><\/code>\u00a0must be the first two characters of the file. On some platforms, this first line must end with a Unix-style line ending ('\\n'<\/span><\/code>), not a Windows ('\\r\\n'<\/span><\/code>) line ending. Note that the hash, or pound, character,\u00a0'#'<\/span><\/code>, is used to start a comment in Python.<\/p>
                                    The script can be given an executable mode, or permission, using the\u00a0chmod<\/strong>\u00a0command.<\/p>
                                    $<\/span> chmod +x myscript.py\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    On Windows systems, there is no notion of an \u201cexecutable mode\u201d. The Python installer automatically associates\u00a0.py<\/span><\/code>\u00a0files with\u00a0python.exe<\/span><\/code>\u00a0so that a double-click on a Python file will run it as a script. The extension can also be\u00a0.pyw<\/span><\/code>, in that case, the console window that normally appears is suppressed.<\/p><\/div>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    16.1.3.\u00a0<\/span>The Interactive Startup File<\/h3>
                                    When you use Python interactively, it is frequently handy to have some standard commands executed every time the interpreter is started. You can do this by setting an environment variable named\u00a0<\/span>PYTHONSTARTUP<\/span><\/code>\u00a0to the name of a file containing your start-up commands. This is similar to the\u00a0.profile<\/span><\/code>\u00a0feature of the Unix shells.<\/p>
                                    This file is only read in interactive sessions, not when Python reads commands from a script, and not when\u00a0\/dev\/tty<\/span><\/code>\u00a0is given as the explicit source of commands (which otherwise behaves like an interactive session). It is executed in the same namespace where interactive commands are executed, so that objects that it defines or imports can be used without qualification in the interactive session. You can also change the prompts\u00a0sys.ps1<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0sys.ps2<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in this file.<\/p>
                                    If you want to read an additional start-up file from the current directory, you can program this in the global start-up file using code like\u00a0if<\/span>\u00a0os.path.isfile('.pythonrc.py'):<\/span>\u00a0exec(open('.pythonrc.py').read())<\/span><\/code>. If you want to use the startup file in a script, you must do this explicitly in the script:<\/p>
                                    import<\/span> os<\/span>\nfilename<\/span> =<\/span> os<\/span>.<\/span>environ<\/span>.<\/span>get<\/span>(<\/span>'PYTHONSTARTUP'<\/span>)<\/span>\nif<\/span> filename<\/span> and<\/span> os<\/span>.<\/span>path<\/span>.<\/span>isfile<\/span>(<\/span>filename<\/span>):<\/span>\n    with<\/span> open<\/span>(<\/span>filename<\/span>)<\/span> as<\/span> fobj<\/span>:<\/span>\n        startup_file<\/span> =<\/span> fobj<\/span>.<\/span>read<\/span>()<\/span>\n    exec<\/span>(<\/span>startup_file<\/span>)<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div><\/div>
                                    \u00a0<\/p>
                                    16.1.4.\u00a0<\/span>The Customization Modules<\/h3>
                                    Python provides two hooks to let you customize it:\u00a0sitecustomize<\/span><\/code>\u00a0and\u00a0usercustomize<\/span><\/code>. To see how it works, you need first to find the location of your user site-packages directory. Start Python and run this code:<\/p>
                                    >>><\/span><\/p>
                                    >>> <\/span>import<\/span> site<\/span>\n>>> <\/span>site<\/span>.<\/span>getusersitepackages<\/span>()<\/span>\n'\/home\/user\/.local\/lib\/python3.5\/site-packages'<\/span>\n<\/pre><\/div><\/div>
                                    Now you can create a file named\u00a0usercustomize.py<\/span><\/code>\u00a0in that directory and put anything you want in it. It will affect every invocation of Python, unless it is started with the\u00a0-s<\/span><\/code>\u00a0option to disable the automatic import.<\/p>
                                    sitecustomize<\/span><\/code>\u00a0works in the same way, but is typically created by an administrator of the computer in the global site-packages directory, and is imported before\u00a0usercustomize<\/span><\/code>. See the documentation of the\u00a0site<\/span><\/code>\u00a0module for more details.<\/p><\/div><\/div>\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t\t\t\t<\/div>\n\t\t<\/section>\n\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t
                                    \n\t\t\t\t\t
